This document is organized into\n\nPart I - Address perspectives\non ocean color research and validation,\nand requirements\nfor in situ\nobservations\n(Chapter 1 and Chapter 2).\nPart H - Address the characteristics\nand performance\nspecifications\nof the instruments used for in situ\nobservation,\nand provide protocols for\nPart H - Provide detailed protocols\nand associated data analysis (Chapters\nPart IV - Address the methods and\nquality\n\ncontrol\n\n(Chapter\n\ntheir calibration and characterization\n(Chapters 3 through 10).\ndescribing methods for making each type of field measurement\n11 through 14).\nprocedures\nfor data archival, data synthesis and merging, and\n\n15).\n\nThis document marks a significant departure from, and improvement\non, the format and content of Mueller\nand Austin (1995).\nThe authorship\nof the protocols\nhas been greatly broadened\nto include experts\nspecializing\nin some key areas.\nNew chapters have been added to provide detailed and comprehensive\nprotocols\nfor stability monitoring\nof radiometers\nusing portable sources (Chapter 7 by Hooker),\nabovewater measurements\nof remote-sensing\nreflectance\n(Chapter\n10 by Mueller et al.), spectral absorption\nmeasurements\nfor discrete water samples (Chapter\n12 by Mitchell et al.), HPLC pigment analysis (Chapter\n13 by Bidigare and Trees) and fluorometric\npigment analysis (Chapter 14 by Trees et al.). Protocols were\nincluded in Mueller and Austin (1995) for each of these areas, but the new treatment makes significant\nadvances\nin each topic area.\nThere are also new chapters prescribing\nprotocols\nfor calibration\nof sun\nphotometers\nand sky radiance sensors (Chapter 6 by Pietras et al.), sun photometer\nand sky radiance\nmeasurements\nand analysis (Chapter 11 by Frouin et al.), and data archival (Chapter\n15 by Werdell et al.).\nThese topic areas were barely mentioned in Mueller and Austin (1995).\nThe present status of the protocols\nis less encouraging\nwith respect to radiometric\nmeasurements\nfrom\nmoored and drifting buoys, methods of inherent optical properties\nmeasurement\nand calibration,\nand\nairborne measurements.\nThere have been rapid and significant\nadvances in each of these areas over the\npast five years.\nUnfortunately,\nother commitments\nof key scientists specializing\nin these areas made it\nimpractical\nto carry out the discussions needed to establish a consensus on draft protocols within the short\ntime allowed for preparation\nand publication\nof this document.\nThe status of methods in these topic areas\nare briefly reviewed\nin Chapters 3 and 10, together with protocols\nfor computing\nNormalized\nWaterLeaving Radiance\n(including\nBRDF effects) and for measuring\nancillary\nvariables.\nDeveloping\nnew\nchapters describing comprehensive,\nup-to-date protocols in each of these areas is a high priority for future\nrevisions to this document.\nThis technical report is not meant as a substitute for scientific literature, Instead, it will provide a ready and\nresponsive\nvehicle for the multitude of technical reports issued by an operational\nProject. Fargion\nSan\n\nCorporation,\nFlight\n\n1.1 INTRODUCTION\nThe Ocean\nOptics\nProtocols\nfor Satellite\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n(Version 2.0) are\nintended\nto provide\nstandards,\nwhich if followed\ncarefully and documented\nappropriately,\nwill assure\nthat any particular\nset of optical measurements\nwill\nbe acceptable\nfor ocean color sensor validation and\nalgorithm\ndevelopment.\nThese\nprotocols\nare\nguidelines\nand may be somewhat conservative.\nIn\nthe case of ship shadow avoidance,\nfor example,\nthere are some circumstances\nin which acceptable\nradiometric\nprofiles may be acquired considerably\ncloser to a ship than is specified\nhere (Section\n10.1). When the protocols are not followed in such\ncases,\nhowever,\nit is incumbent\nupon\nthe\ninvestigator\nto explicitly demonstrate that the actual\nerror levels are within tolerance. Close adherence to\nthese protocols\nis the most straightforward\nway for\nan investigator\nto establish\na measurement\nthat is\nuncontaminated\nby artifacts, such as ship shadow,\nand is accurate enough to meet the requirements\nof\nsatellite ocean color product validation.\nFinally, having a standard set of measurement\nprotocols is indispensible\nin developing consistency\nacross the variety of international satellite ocean\ncolor\nmissions\neither\nrecently\nlaunched\nor\nscheduled\nfor launch in the next few years. While\neach mission\nhas its own validation\neffort, the\nmission validation\nteams should not need to define\nseparate validation\nmeasurement\nrequirements.\nIn\nthe U.S., for instance,\nocean\ncolor validation\nsupport\nis derived\nfrom four separate\nfunding\nprograms, i.e., the Sea-viewing\nWide Field-of-view\nSensor\n(SeaWiFS)\nProject,\nModerate\nResolution\nImaging\nSpectroradiometer\n(MODIS)\nvalidation\nprogram,\nthe Earth\nObserving\nSystem\n(EOS)\ncalibration\nand validation\nprogram, and the Sensor\nIntercomparison\nfor\nMarine\nBiology\nand\nInterdisciplinary\nOceanic\nStudies\n(SIMBIOS)\nProject (McClain and Fargion, 1999 a and b).\n\nCenter,\n\nDiego\n\n2 and\nState\n\nCharles\nUniversity,\n\nBeltsvilIe,\n\n3\n\nCalifornia\n\nMaryland\n\nGreenbelt,\n\nR. McClain\n\nMaryland\n\nContinued\ndevelopment\nand\nthese\nprotocols\nhelp\nensure\ncollaboration,\nand communication\ninvolved.\n\nrefinement\nof\ncoordination,\nbetween\nthose\n\n1.2 OBJECTIVES\nImmediate\nconcerns focused previous versions\nof the Ocean Optics Protocols (Mueller and Austin\n1992,\n1995)\non specific\npreparations\nfor the\nSeaWiFS\nmission.\nIn the\ninterim,\nnot\nonly\nSeaWiFS,\nbut\nthe\nJapanese\nOcean\nColor\nTemperature\nSensor\n(OCTS),\nthe Polarization\nDetection\nEnvironmental\nRadiometer\n(POLDER),\nand the MODIS\nglobal\ncoverage\nocean\ncolor\nsystems\nhave been successfully\nlaunched\nand\nbrought into operation, and the near-term launch of\nseveral other such systems is anticipated\n(Appendix\nA). The SIMBIOS\nProgram goal is to assist the\ninternational\nocean color community\nin developing\na multi-year time-series of calibrated radiances that\ntranscends\nthe spatial and temporal boundaries of\nindividual missions. Specific objectives\nare to: (1)\nquantify\nthe relative\naccuracies\nof the products\nfrom each mission, (2) work with each project to\nimprove the level Of confidence\nand compatibility\namong the products, and (3) develop methodologies\nfor generating\nmerged level-3 products.\nSIMBIOS\nhas identified\nthe primary instruments\nto be used\nfor developing\nglobal data sets. These instruments\nare SeaWiFS,\nOCTS, POLDER\n[Advanced\nEarth\nObserving\nSatellite (ADEOS)-I\nand II], MODIS\n(Terra\nand\nAqua),\nMulti-angle\nImaging\nSpectroRadiometer\nMISR,\nMedium\nResolution\nImaging Spectrometer\n(MERIS),\nand Global Line\nImager (GLI).\nThe products from other missions\n[e.g., Ocean Color Imager (OCI), Ocean Scanning\nMultisprectral\nImager\n(OSMI),\nand\nModular\nOptoelectronic\nScanner (MOS)] will be tracked and\nevaluated,\nbut are not considered\nas key data\nsources for a combined global data set.\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean Sensor\nO Color\nValidation\nThe scopeof the present ersionof the 5.\nv\nprotocolsis, therefore, roadened support\nb\nto\ndevelopmentbio-optical\nof\ndatabases\nthatmeet\nthe\nexapanded\nrequirements\nof theSIMBIOS and\ngoals\nobjectives.Thekey objective\naddressed the\nby\noriginal working group was to recommend\nprotocols\nand standards supporting situ 6.\nfor\nin\noptical\nmeasurements.\nThe\noriginal\nobjectives\nremain\nvalid\ntoday,\nalbeit\nwith\nbroader\nrequirements\nfor detailed measurements\nand sensor\ncharacteristics\n(e.g. Wavelength\ncharacteristics).\nThe generalized\nprotocol\nobjectives\naddress\nthe\nfollowing subject areas:\n1. The required and useful optical parameters\nto\nbe used for validation\nof satellite ocean color\nsensor normalized\nwater-leaving\nradiances and\natmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms,\nand for\nmonitoring\neach satellite\nsensor\'s\ncalibration\nand stability, will be defined.\n2.\nThe\ninstrumentation\nrequirements,\nand\nstandards for measuring the parameters\nin item\n1, including definitions of measured quantities,\nwavelengths,\nfield-of-view\n(FOV)\nand band\nspecifications,\nsensitivity,\nuncertainty\nand\nstability, will be delineated.\n3.\nThe\noptical\ninstrument\ncharacterization,\nintercalibration\nstandards, and related protocols\nwill be defined.\nThis objective\nincludes\nthe\nfollowing subjects:\na) laboratory\ncalibration\nand characterization\nmeasurements,\nuncertainties,\nand\nprocedures\nused\nin\nvalidation\nactivities;\nb)\n\nc)\n\n4.\n\nto be applied\nsatellite\nocean\nand\nalgorithm\n\nb)\n\nc)\n\nSpecific\nmethods\nfor\ndevelopment\nand\nvalidation of bio-optical\nalgorithms for ocean color\nsensors are only briefly examined\nin this report.\nNonetheless,\nthe scope of the optics protocols\nincludes data requirements\nand sampling strategies\nfor bio-optical\nand\nradiometric\nmeasurements\nsupporting\nthese activities.\nThis topic includes the\nfollowing subjects:\n1. Discrete\nchlorophyll\na\nand\npigment\nconcentrations\nwill be measured using for\nhigh performance\nliquid chromatography\n(HPLC)\npigment\nsampling\nand analysis,\nprotocols\nand standards for which closely\nfollow those adopted by the Joint Global\nOcean Flux Study (JGOFS)\n(UNESCO\n1994).\n2.\nAn assessment will be made of the roles of\nunderway,\nmoored,\nand\ndiscrete\nfluorescence\nmeasurements,\nhow\nsuch\nmeasurements\nare calibrated,\nand their\nusefulness\nfor\nsatellite\ndata\nproduct\nvalidation.\nProtocols\nare included\nfor\n\nto instruments\ncolor\nsensor\ndevelopment\n\npre- and post-deployment\nmeasurements\nand procedures\nto be followed\nwith\nmoored instrumentation;\nand\nmethods\nfor instrument\ncalibration\nand\ncharacterization,\nand the requirements\nfor\nrecord keeping and traceability,\nincluding\nintercalibrations\nof radiometric\nand optical\nstandards\nbetween\nparticipating\nlaboratories.\n\nThe at-sea\noptical\nsampling\nstrategy\nand\nprotocols\nwill be standardized.\nThis objective\nincludes such considerations\nas:\na)\n\n7.\n\nThe analysis approaches\nto be used shall be\nrefined. This objective includes procedures\nand\nmethodologies\nrecommended\nfor generating\nvariables from in situ observations,\ne.g., L wt_\n(z)\nfrom\nLu\n(z),\nK(z),\nremote\nsensing\nreflectance, etc., as well as error analysis.\nProtocols\nfor ancillary\nmeasurements,\ndata\narchiving,\ndatabase population,\nand access to\ndata will be standardized.\nThe required atfiaospheric\nmeasurements\nwill\nbe defined, and the degree to which standard\nmethodologies\nare available will be evaluated.\n\nthe rationale and justifications\nfor moored,\nunderway,\ndrifting,\nshipboard,\nand\nairborne measurements;\nship shadow avoidance, depth resolution in\noptical profiles, and total sampling depths;\nand\ntime of day, sky conditions,\nseason, and\ngeographic\nconsiderations.\n\n3.\n\nfluorometric\nmeasurement\nof chlorophyll\na\nconcentration,\nagain closely following the\ncounterpart\nJGOFS\nprotocols\n(UNESCO\n1994).\nThe\nneed\nfor\nbiogeochemical\nmeasurements\nof\ncolored\ndissolved\norganic\nmaterial\n(CDOM),\ncoccoliths,\nsuspended\nsediment, detritus, etc., will be\nexamined on the basis of baseline product\nrequirements.\nProtocols are included here\nfor in situ and laboratory\nmeasurements\nof\nspectral\nabsorption\nby CDOM,\nand by\nsuspended\nparticles.\nThe other aspects of\nthis topic are addressed\nin more general\nterms.\n\n1.3 SENSOR\n\nCALIBRATION\n\nThe individual\nsatellite sensor project offices,\nas well as the SIMBIOS Project, must make every\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor ensor\nO\nC S\nValidation\neffortto trackthesensor\'s\nperformance\nthroughout fields in\nthe duration the mission.\nof\nSince\nSeaWiFS, distribution\nfor\nexample, designed five-year\nis\nfora\nmission, was as well\nit\ncertain\nfromtheoutset\nthatthesensor\ncalibration spanning\nat\neach wavelengthwould change in some evaluation\nunpredictable\nmanneras a functionof time. products\nExperience ith Coastal Zone Color Scanner\nw\nconsistent\n(CZCS)\nsensor\'s\n\nhas shown it is very difficult to determine a\ncalibration\nonce it has been launched\n\n(Viollier\n1982, Gordon et al. 1983, Hovis et al.\n1985, Mueller 1985, Gordon 1987, and Evans and\nGordon\n1994).\nSimilar\nproblems\nhave\nbeen\nencountered\nwith other earth observing\nsystems,\nsuch as the National\nOceanic\nand Atmospheric\nAdministration\n(NOAA)\nAdvanced\nVery\nHigh\nResolution\nRadiometer\n(AVHRR)\n(Brown\nand\nEvans 1985 and Weinreb et al. 1990). Because of\nthe large atmospheric\ncontribution\nto the total\nobserved\nradiances\n(Gordon\n1981) and the great\nsensitivity\nof the bio-optical\nalgorithms\nto the\nestimated\nwater-leaving\nradiances\n(Clark\n1981),\nsmall errors in the calibration\ncan induce sizable\nerrors in derived\ngeophysical\nproducts,\nrendering\nthem useless for many applications.\nBy processing\nlarge quantities\nof so-called\n"clear\nwater _ imagery,\ni.e., water with pigment\nconcentrations\nless than 0.25 mg m 3 (Gordon and\nClark 1981), Evans and Gordon (1994) were able to\ndevelop a vicarious calibration that was used in the\nglobal processing\nof the entire CZCS data set\n(Esaias\net al. 1986, Feldman\net al. 1989 and\nMcClain\net al. 1993). The approach,\nhowever,\nrequires assumptions\nthat may limit the scientific\nutility of ocean color imagery.\nSpecifically,\nthe\nnormalized\nclear\nwater-leaving\nradiances,\nLWN\n(443), LWN (520), and LWN (550), were assumed to\nbe\n\n1.40,\n\n0.48,\n\nand\n\n0.30\n\nmW\n\ncm -2 I_m_\n\nsr 1,\n\nrespectively.\nThe\nAngstrom\nexponents\nwere\nassumed\nto be zero and certain\ngeographical\nregions such as the Sargasso Sea were assumed to\nbe clear water sites at all times. Under\nthese\nassumptions,\nthe clear-water\n(LwN) values were\nused to calculate calibration adjustment coefficients\nto bring CZCS derived (LwN) values into agreement\nfor these regions. The vicarious calibration\nof the\n443 nm band is tenuous,\nbecause\nof the great\nvariability\nin LwN(443)\neven in clear\nwater.\nAdditionally,\ncertain\ncommand\nand engineering\ndata from\nthe NIMBUS-7\nplatform\nwere not\narchived,\nso that a detailed analysis of possible\neffects related to the spacecraft\nenvironment\nand\nthe effects of spacecraft operation on the calibration\ncould not be performed.\nUnlike\nCZCS,\nSeaWiFS\nand other modern\nocean\n\ncolor\n\nsensors\n\nroutinely\n\nproduce\n\ngeophysical\n\na near-real\ntime, operational\nmode for\nto the science community.\nThis aspect,\nas merger\nof multi-satellite\ndata sets\nmany\nyears,\nnecessitates\nconstant\nof system performance\nand derived\nfor all of the sensors.\nTherefore,\na\nmultifaceted\napproach\nto\naddress\nproblems\nof sensitivity\ndegradation\nand sensor\ncharacterization\nis required on a continuing\nbasis.\nThe goal is to ensure that satellite derived waterleaving radiances\nare accurately\nknown and meet\nthe specifications\nof the individual\nmissions\nand\nSIMBIOS.\nAs implemented\nby the SeaWiFS\nProject\nOffice (SPO), for example, the validation\nprogram\nincludes\nboth onboard\nand vicarious\ncalibration\napproaches\n(McClain\net al. 1998, Barnes et al.\n1999a and McClain\net al. 2000a and 2000b).\nSeaWiFS has a solar measuring\ndiffuser plate to\nreference the response to the sun and is also capable\nof periodically\nimaging the moon by maneuvering\nthe spacecraft\n(Barnes et aI. 1999b). MODIS and\nsome other\nocean\ncolor\nsensors\nhave similar\ncapabilities.\nThe vicarious\ncalibration\nprogram\nincorporates\nmeasurements\nof\nwater-leaving\nradiances, and other related quantities,\nfrom ships,\ndrifting buoys, and fixed moorings, to develop time\nseries\nand geographically\ndiverse\nsamples\nof\noceanic and atmospheric\ndata. Each approach has\nadvantages and disadvantages,\nbut when combined,\nthey\nshould\nprovide\na\ncomplementary\nand\ncomprehensive\ndata set that will be sufficient\nto\nmonitor short-term changes and long-term trends in\nthe sensor\'s performance.\nPresently,\nthe\nSIMBIOS\nProject\nuses\na\ncombination\nof satellite and in situ observations\nfrom geographically\ndiverse vicarious\ncalibration\ntest sites as a means of comparing\nocean color\nsatellite\ninstruments.\nUsing\nthis\nvicarious\ncalibration\napproach,\nresults\nretrieved\nfrom\ndifferent\nsensors can be meaningfully\ncompared\nand possibly merged.\nMore importantly,\none can\nuse the same procedure,\nwith in situ ocean and\natmospheric\noptical\nproperty\nmeasurements,\nto\nrecalibrate satellite sensors (Fargion et al., 1999).\nThe SIMBIOS calibration\nstrategy is to focus\non regions and circumstances\nwhere the optical\nproperties of the marine atmosphere\nand ocean are\nwell understood\nand homogeneous,\ni.e., where the\nerrors in the atmospheric\ncorrection\nand the in situ\noptical measurements\nare expected to be minimal.\nThe Marine Optical Buoy (MOBY)\n(Clark et al.\n1997), near the island of Lanai, Hawaii, provides\nthe principal\ninstrumented\ntest site for vicarious\ncalibration\nmeasurements.\nThe\nMOBY\nproject\nofficially\nsupports the validation\nof ocean color\n\nOcean\nOptics\n\nProtocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\ndata that is collected by SeaWiFS and MODIS. In\naddition,\nMOBY has been successfully\nused for\nOCTS\nand POLDER\nand indirectly\nfor MOS\n(Wang and Franz, 1999) vicarious calibrations.\n\n1.4 BIO-OPTICAL\nALGORITHMS\nThe SPO, and each of the counterpart\nocean\ncolor sensor projects, is responsible for producing a\nstandard\nset of derived\nproducts.\nThe oceanic\nproducts include chlorophyll\nconcentration,\nK(490),\nand five normalized water-leaving\nradiances.\nThe basic quantities to be computed from the\nsensor radiances\nare the water-leaving\nradiances,\nfrom which all other derived products except the\naerosol products are computed.\nEvery effort must\nbe made to ensure\nthese radiances\nmeet the\nspecifications,\n\n+\n\n5%\n\nin\n\nCase-I\n\nwaters.\n\nThis\n\nrequires\nthe atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\nto\nbe considerably\nmore sophisticated\nthan were the\noriginal CZCS algorithms.\nThe baseline bio-optical\nproducts\nmust meet\nthe\nSeaWiFS,\nMODIS,\nother\nsensors,\nand\nSIMBIOS\nProject\naccuracy\nrequirements\nover a\nvariety of water masses.\nThe CZCS algorithms\nwere based on a data set consisting of fewer than 50\ndata points (only 14 observations\nwere available for\nthe\nband-2-to-band-3\nratio\nalgorithm)\nand\nperformed\npoorly in regions of high concentrations\nof phytoplankton\npigments, suspended sediment, or\nCDOM,\nand in coccolithophorid\nblooms (Groom\nand Holligan\n1987). Accurate\nestimates\nof the\nbaseline products are essential if SeaWiFS is to be\nuseful\nin programs\nsuch as JGOFS\n[National\nAcademy\nof Science\n(NAS)\n1984] and climate\nchange research.\nSeaWiFS,\nand the other modern ocean color\nsensors, have the capability,\ndue to improvements\nin the signal-to-noise\nratio (SNR),\ndigitization,\ndynamic\nrange,\nand\nwavelength\nselection,\nto\nincrease the accuracy of these products and to flag\nareas\nwhere\nanomalies\nor\nlow\nconfidence\nconditions\nexist. Clearly, a much larger database is\nneeded\nfor developing\nand validating\na broader\nvariety of bio-optical\nalgorithms,\nsome of which\nwill be region specific.\nThe radiometric,\noptical,\nand chemical\nfield observations\nused in deriving\nbio-optical\nalgorithms and for vicarious calibration\nof the sensor must, therefore, conform to stringent,\nuniform\nrequirements\nwith respect\nto instrument\ncalibration\nand characterization,\nand methods of\nobservation.\n\nOcean Color Sensor\n\nValidation\n\nThe SeaWiFS and SIMBIOS\nProjects manage\na program\nto compare\nthe various atmospheric\ncorrection\nand bio-optical\nalgorithms\nproposed\nby\nthe science community\n(Wang and Bailey 2000,\nMcClain et al. 2000a and b, O\'Reilly et al., 2000).\nThe purpose of this program is to independently\nevaluate suggested\nimprovements,\nor additions,\nto\nthe SeaWiFS and merged products. This component\nof the calibration\nand algorithm\ndevelopment\nprogram runs in parallel\nwith, but off-line\nfrom,\noperational\nprocessing\nand provides\nan essential\nmechanism\nfor incorporating\ndata and analyses\nfrom the community at large.\n\n1.5 VICARIOUS\n\nCALIBRATION\n\nFor ocean observations,\nit is easy to show\n(Gordon\n1987 and Gordon\n1988) that satellite\nsensor calibration requirements\nbased on the quality\nof the existing CZCS pigment algorithms\nexceed\ncurrently\navailable\ncapabilities.\nFurthermore,\nthe\nsensor calibration\nis unlikely to remain unchanged\nthrough launch and five years of operation in orbit.\nThe only foreseeable way of approaching\nthe ocean\ncalibration\nneeds is through vicarious calibration,\ni.e., fine tuning the calibration in orbit.\nThe methodology\nused to achieve\nvicarious\ncalibration\nfor CZCS was described\nin detail by\nGordon (1987). First, the calibration was initialized\nafter launch by forcing\nagreement\nbetween\nthe\nsensor-determined\nradiance\nand\nthe\nexpected\nradiance based on radiometric\nmeasurements\nmade\nat the surface under clear atmospheric\nconditions.\nNext, since the CZCS responsivity\nwas observed to\nbe time dependent,\nthe algorithms\nwere applied to\nother scenes characterized\nby bio-optical\nsurface\nmeasurements\nand more typical atmospheres,\nand\nthe calibration\nwas adjusted\nuntil the measured\nwater-leaving\nradiances\nwere reproduced.\nFinally,\nthe\nsurface\nmeasurements\nof pigments\nwere\ncombined\nwith satellite\npigment estimates\nfor a\nwide variety of atmospheric\nconditions,\nand the\nradiance calibration\nwas fine tuned until the best\nagreement was obtained between the retrieved and\ntrue pigments.\nThe CZCS\nvicarious\ncalibration\nwas not\nradiometric.\nIt was a calibration\nof the entire\nsystem-sensor\nplus algorithms.\nTo predict\nthe\nradiance measured\nat the satellite,\nLt, the waterleaving radiance, the aerosol optical thickness, and\nthe aerosol phase function are all required.\nAlso\nneeded\nare ancillary\ndata such as the surface\npressure, wind speed, and ozone optical thickness.\nThese data for vicarious calibration\nand validation\nwill\n\nbe\n\nobtained\n\nby\n\nmeasuring\n\nthe\n\nupwelling\n\nOcean ptics rotocols Satellite\nO\nP\nFor\nradiance distribution\njust beneath the surface, along\nwith the aerosol\noptical\nthickness\nand the sky\nradiance, at the time of the satellite overpass (Clark\net al. 1997). The sky radiance\nwill be used to\ndeduce the required information\nabout the aerosol\nphase function (Voss and Zibordi 1989). The data\nset will be used to deduce Lt, at the top of the\natmosphere,\ncoincident\nwith a SeaWiFS\noverpass\nfrom which the calibration will be initialized.\nThe present approach\nused by the SIMBIOS\nand SeaWiFS Projects\nis to develop a Level-lb to\nLevel-2\nsoftware\npackage\n(MSI12)\nwhich\nis\ncapable\nof processing\ndata from multiple\nocean\ncolor\nsensors\nusing\nthe\nstandard\nSeaWiFS\natmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\nof Gordon and\nWang (1994a, b). The integration\nof a new sensor\ninto MSII2\ninvolves the development\nof a set of\ninput functions and derivation of band-pass specific\nquantities\nsuch as Rayleigh\nscattering\ntables and\nRayleigh-aerosol\ntransmittance\ntables.\nOnce the\nprocessing\ncapability\nhas been established,\nthe\nvicarious calibration\ncan be tuned using match-up\ndata from the MOBY site, and/or cross calibration\nwith another sensor. For example, Wang and Franz\n(1999) used SeaWiFS normalized\nremote sensing\nreflectances\nand aerosol models to successfully\nrecalibrate the MOS spectral channels.\nUsing this approach, the SIMBIOS Project can\nprovide\na completely\nindependent\nassessment\nof\ninstrument calibration\nand sensor-to-sensor\nrelative\ncalibration.\nThe Project\nsensor teams\non how\n\nalso provides insight to the\ndifferences\nin calibration\n\ntechniques\nand atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\npropagate\nthrough\nthe processing\nto produce\ndifferences\nin retrieved\noptical properties\nof the\nwater. It must be stressed that this exercise\nis\nabsolutely\nessential for calibrating\nthe ocean color\nsystems, i.e., sensors plus algorithms,\nand that it\ncannot\nbe implemented\nwithout\na high quality\nsurface data set obtained\nsimultaneously\nwith the\nsatellite imagery.\n\n1.6 AEROSOL\nTHICKNESS\n\nOPTICAL\nVALIDATION\n\nAerosol optical thickness products determined\nfrom the satellite ocean color data itself are critical\nfactors in the uncertainty\nbudgets of atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\n(Gordon and Wang 1994a)\nand results of vicarious\ncalibrations\n(Clark et al.\n1997; Gordon 1981, 1987, 1988).\nThe SIMBIOS\nProject is validating\nthe SeaWiFS aerosol optical\nproducts\nby\ncomparing\nthem\nto\nin\nsitu\nmeasurements\n(Wang\net al., 2000).\nA second,\nrelated\nobjective\nof these\ncomparisons\nis to\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\ndetermine\nthe validity\nof the aerosol\nmodels\ncurrently\nused\nby\nSeaWiFS\nfor atmospheric\ncorrection.\nThe\nprincipal\nsource\nof in situ\naerosol\nobservations\nis the Aerosol\nRobotic\nNetwork\n(AERONET).\nAERONET\nis a network of groundbased\nautomated\nsun photometers\nowned\nby\nnational\nagencies\nand universities\n(Holben\net al.\n1998).\nAERONET\ndata\nprovides\nglobally\ndistributed,\nnear-real time observations\nof aerosol\nspectral optical depths, aerosol size distributions,\nand precipitable\nwater. Because the majority of the\nAERONET\nstations\nare at continental\nlocations,\nSIMBIOS\naugmented\nthe\nnetwork\nwith\n12\nadditional\nisland and coastal sites, including Lanai\nand Oahu Hawaii,\nAscension\nIsland,\nBahrain,\nTahiti,\nWallops\nIsland,\nSouth\nKorea,\nTurkey,\nArgentina,\nAzores\nand Perth. SIMBIOS\nProject\nalso has shipboard\nhand-held\nsun photometers\n(MicroTops,\nPREDE, SIMBAD and Lidar). These\ninstruments are calibrated in collaboration\nwith the\nAERONET\nProgram\nat NASA\nGoddard\nSpace\nFlight Center (GSFC) and loaned to investigators\nstaging SIMBIOS\nsponsored research expeditions.\n\n1.7 COMMUNITY\nPARTICIPATION\nThe SeaWiFS\nand SIMBIOS\nProject Offices\nrely on the oceanographic\ncommunity\nto perform\nfield research\nfor atmospheric\nand bio-optical\nalgorithm development,\nand for all of the in situ\ndata collection for the vicarious sensor calibration.\nThe SIMBIOS\nProject sponsors a subset of these\nobservations,\nbut many projects sponsored\nby the\nNASA Research\nand Application\nProgram,\nother\ngovernment\nagencies and the international\nocean\ncolor\nresearch\ncommunity\nall\nmake\nmajor\ncontributions\nto the global multi-year effort.\nThe SIMBIOS\nProject\nhas undertaken\nthe\nchallenge of coordinating\nthe in situ observations\ncontributed\nby these various programs, linking it to\nocean\ncolor\nimagery\nfrom\nthe\ninternational\nensemble\nof satellite\nsensors,\nand making\nthe\noverall\ndata sets available\nto the ocean\ncolor\nresearch\ncommunity\n(McClain\nand\nFargion\n1999a,b).\nA workable\nstrategy\nto meet those\nchallenges\nfirst requires\na clear definition\nof the\nobservations,\nuncertainties,\nand data collection\nprotocols associated with each type of activity. The\npurpose\nof this document\nis to clarify\nthese\nrequirements.\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor ensor\nO\nC S\nValidation\n1.8 PROTOCOL\nORGANIZATION\n\nDOCUMENT\n\nEvans, R.H., and H.R. Fargion\n\nFlight\n\nThe principal in situ variables to be measured,\nor derived from measurements,\nfor satellite ocean\ncolor sensor validation,\nand algorithm development\nand validation, are listed in Table 2. l. The variables\nare grouped, in Table 2.1, into four related groups:\nRadiometric\nQuantities\n(both\noceanic\nand\natmospheric),\nInherent Optical Properties\n(lOP) of\nsea\nwater,\nBiogeochemicai\nand\nBio-Optical\nProperties\nof sea water, and Ancillary\nData and\nMetadata\nrequired\nto support\nthe use, analysis,\ninterpretation,\nand quality assessment\nof the other\ndata. Those in situ variables that are measured are\nclassified\ninto three\ncategories\nof descending\npriority.\nThe first category\nof measurements,\nflagged\n"Required"\nin Table 2.1, is the minimum\nsubset\nrequired\nfor\nvalidating\na\nsatellite\nsensor\'s\nradiometric\nperformance,\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiances,\nand\nfundamental\nderived\nproducts,\nincluding\nchlorophyll\na concentration,\naerosol\noptical thickness,\nand K(490), and for associated\nalgorithm development\nand validation.\nThe second category, flagged "Highly Desired"\nin Table 2.1, are measurements\nthat supplement the\nminimum subset and are needed for investigations\nfocused on atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms and\naerosols,\nrelationships\nbetween\nlOP and remote\nsensing reflectance,\nand/or Case 2 algorithms.\nThe\nthird\ncategory,\nflagged\n"Specialized\nMeasurement"\nin Table 2.1, are measurements\nwhich either address aspects of ocean bio-optics\nthat are secondary\nto satellite remote sensing,\' or\nrequire\nhighly specialized\nequipment\nthat is not\nrefidil3_tvailable\nto the community at large.\nA fourth\ncategory,\nflagged\nas "Derived",\ncomprises\nkey quantities that are either calculated\nfrom the in situ measurements,\nor are derived from\nmodels.\nThe above set of variables is also listed in\nthe satellite\n\nocean color\n\n2 and\n\nSensing,\nSan\nCorporation,\n\n2.1INTRODUCTION\n\nTable 2.2, to identify\n\n2\n\nCenter,\n\nCharles\n\nR. Lw(_.) is, in turn, adjusted using\nEs(_.) to derive\nthe normalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance, LwNQ.), for a no-atmosphere,\nzenith sun\nat the mean Earth-sun distance. Unfortunately,\nit is\nnot practical\nto measure\nLu(0,_.) precisely\nat an\ninfinitesimal\ndepth below the surface. Therefore,\nthe profile of Lu(z,X), must be measured over the\nupper few optical depths with sufficient accuracy to\ndetermine\nKL(Z,7_) for L.(z, _), and to propagate\nLu(z,_.) to the surface.\nAt near-infrared\n(NIR)\nwavelengths,\nthe first optical attenuation\nlength is\nconfined\nto the upper few tens of centimeters.\nDetermination\nof Lu(0-,_.), in this situation is more\nchallenging\nand will require\nspecial instruments\nand experiment\ndesigns to accommodate\nthe effects\nof instrument\nself-shading,\nwave focusing, smallscale\nvariability,\npossible\nfluorescence,\nRaman\nscattering,\nand extremely\nsmall working volumes.\nSimilar complications\narise at all wavelengths\nin\nCase-2\nwaters.\nFor algorithm\ndevelopment\nand\nvalidation in these difficult cases, measurements\nof\ninherent optical properties\n(lOPs), including a(z,L),\nc(z,_.) and bb(z,k),\nand spectral fluorescence,\nmay\nbe usefully\ncombined\nwith Ea(z,_.), and Lu (z,_.)\nmeasured\nwith specially designed radiometers\nand\nLsfc(_.,0,_,0o,_o)\nand Lsky(_,,0,_,0o,_o)\nmeasured\nabove-water.\nUpwelled\nspectral\nirradiance,\nE,(z,k)\n, is a\nuseful measurement,\nin addition\nto Ed and L,,\nbecause there exist both empirical and theoretical\nrelationships\nbetween\nIOPs,\nphytoplankton\npigments, TSM, and irradiance reflectance.\nLu(0,_.)\nand E,(0, _.) are related by the factor Q(k), which is\nnot well determined\nat present, and has been shown\nto vary with solar zenith angle (Morel and Gentili\n1993,\n1996; Morel,\nVoss\nand Gentili\n1995).\nCombined\nmeasurements\nof L_(0,_.) and Eu (0,_.)\nwill be extremely useful in determining\nQ(X) which\nwill, in turn, allow traceability\nof the measurements\nby the SIMBIOS\nensemble of satellite ocean color\nsensors to previously\nderived irradiance reflectance\nrelationships\nand algorithms.\nRadiance distribution\nmeasurements\nL(z,_.,0,_)\njust beneath\nthe sea surface will be required for\nquantifying\nthe angular\ndistribution\nof waterleaving\nradiance\nat stations\nused\nfor system\ncalibration\ninitialization\nand long-term\nsystem\ncharacterization.\nThese\nmeasurements\nwill also\nnecessary\nto\ndetermine\nthe\nBidirectional\nReflectance\nDistribution\nFunction\n(BRDF) of the\nwater and verify the models used to normalize\nwater-leaving\nradiance\nfor variations\nin viewing\nand solar zenith angles (Morel and Gentili 1996).\n\nWater\n\nSurface\n\nRadiance\n\n(in\n\nair),\n\nLsfc(_,,0,_,0o,_o),\nmeasured from the deck of a ship\n(or a low-flying\naircraft)\nis a potentially\nuseful\nsubstitute for Lw(L) determined\nfrom in-water L_(0\n,_.). The measured surface radiance is the sum of\nwater-leaving\nradiance and sky radiance\nreflected\nfrom\nthe wave-roughened\nsea surface.\nThe\nprincipal, and significant,\nsource of uncertainty\nin\nthis approach\nis associated\nwith\nremoval\nof\nreflected sky radiance from the total signal.\nSurface incident spectral\nirradiance,\nESQ.), is\nusually measured on a ship well above the water. In\nthe previous versions of these protocols\n(Mueller\nand Austin 1992, 1995), it was suggested that Es(_.)\nmight\nalternatively\nbe\ndetermined\nfrom\nmeasurements of Ed(0",_.) made some distance from\nthe ship using a radiometer floated just beneath the\nsurface.\nThe community\nhas gained experience\nwith this approach\nand found that wave-induced\nfluctuations\nin near-surface\nirradiance\nproduce\nan\nuncertainty\nin Ed(0",k) approaching\n10% in even\nideal cases (Siegel et aI. 1995). Es(_.) varies due to\nfluctuations\nin cloud cover and aerosols, and with\ntime of day, i.e., solar zenith angle. of\nphytoplankton\npigments\nwill emerge in the near\nfuture.\nCoccolith concentration,\nwhich is the number\ndensity of small plates (coccoliths)\ncomposed\nof\ncalcium carbonate\n(CaCO3), is very Coccoliths are produced in copious\namounts\nby\nmarine\nphytoplankton\ncalled\ncoccolithophorids.\nScattering of light by coccoliths\nis highly apparent\nin visible wavelength\nsatellite\nimagery,\nbecause\nthey\nperturb\nthe\nusual\nrelationships\nbetween water-leaving\nradiances and\npigment\nconcentration,\nand therefore,\nadversely\nimpact atmospheric\ncorrections\n(Balch et al. 1991,\nVoss\net\na1.,1998).\nAdditionally,\ncoccolith\nformation,\nsinking, and dissolution\nare significant\nfactors\nin the ocean carbon flux budget. It is,\ntherefore,\nnecessary\nto\nmeasure\ncoccolith\nconcentration,\nboth as number density and CaCO3\nconcentration,\nto aid in 1) the correction\nof\nchlorophyll\na concentration\nalgorithms, 2) coccolith\nalgorithm\ndevelopment,\nand\n3)\natmospheric\ncorrection\ndevelopment\nand validation.\nThis\npresent version (2.0) of the ocean optics protocols\ndoes not cover methods for measuring\ncoccolith\nconcentration.\nSuch protocols may be included in a\nfuture revision.\n\nmeasurements\nof suspended\nTSM is of\nwhere simple\nTSM\nhave\nuncertainties\nequivalent\nto, or greater than, those\nfor\nestimating\nchlorophyll-like\npigment\nconcentration.\nOrganic\nsuspended\nmatter\nand\ninorganic\nsuspended\nmatter\nconcentrations\nare\nsubfractions\nof TSM; this partitioning\nof TSM is\nparticularly\nuseful in process studies.\nContinuous\nprofile measurements\nof in situ\nchlorophyll\na\nfluorescence\nintensity\nare\nexceptionally\nuseful\nas guidance\nin analyzing\nprofiles of Ed(z), Lu(z), and Eo(z) to derive profiles\nof Kd(z), KL(z), and Ko(z), respectively.\nMoreover\nif these profiles are viewed in real time, they are\nalso useful guides\nfor taking water samples\nat\ndepths that allow the vertical structure of pigment\nconcentration\nprofiles to be accurately\nresolved in\nthe top optical depth and subsurface\nchlorophyll\nmaxima. essential\ninformation\nthat must be included\nin every data\nsubmission\nunder the SIMBIOS\nprogram.\nThe\nobvious metadata items in this context are latitude,\nlongitude, date and time (UTC).\nExpressing\ndate\nand time in UTC is also essential, even though it\nmay be helpful to also list local date and time with\na validation\nstation\'s\nmetadata.\nToo often, field\ninvestigators\nneglect to identify (or possibly even\nkeep track of) the time zone used by a PC to enter\ntime into data records.\nSea state, expressed as significant\nwave height\nin m, must be reported\nwith in situ validation\nmeasurements.\nWhitecap\nconditions,\nexpressed\nas\nthe estimated\nfractional\naereal coverage\nare also\nuseful and highly desired.\nDigital photographs\ndocumenting\nsurface wave and whitecap conditions\nduring radiometric\nmeasurements\nare also helpful.\nThis\ninformation\nis essential\nfor identifying\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean Sensor\nO Color\nValidation\nmeasurements\nmade under questionable PROCESS\n2.6\nenvironmental\nconditions.\nRELATED\n\nWind speed and direction\nare required\nto\ngenerate, through models, estimates of the surface\nwave slope distribution,\nwhich will be used to\ncalculate\nreflected\nskylight\nand\nsun glint\nin\nradiative\ntransfer models (Cox and Munk 1954).\nSurface wave models driven by wind velocity may\nalso be used to provide quantitative\nestimates\nof\nsurface\nwave\ninduced\nradiometric\nfluctuations.\n\nOther types of in situ measurements\nare also\nimportant in the context of ocean color validation,\nbecause\nthey are needed either to support,\nor\nvalidate, process models that are derived with the\naid\nof ocean\ncolor\nimage\ndata.\nPrimary\nproductivity\nmodels\nare, perhaps,\nthe foremost\nexample of these secondary\nproducts\nof satellite\nocean\ncolor\nmeasurements.\nThe\nin\nsitu\n\nQualitatively,\nwind velocity,\nand photographs\nor\nvideotape recordings\nof sea state, will be useful for\nassessing station data quality.\nSurface barometric pressure measurements\nare\nrequired\nto validate\nboth atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\nand the surface pressures derived from\noperational\nWeather analyses for use in processing\nsatellite ocean color data\n\nmeasurements\nneeded to support such models, and\nother scientific investigations\nand applications\nthat\nmay exploit\nocean\ncolor\ndata\nproducts,\nare\nundeniably\nimportant\nand closely related\nto the\nquantities listed in Table 2.1. These measurements\nare\nnot,\nhowever,\nessential\nto\nalgorithm\ndevelopment\nand validation\nof products\nderived\nfrom the ocean color data directly.\nIn the future,\nthe scope of the ocean optics protocols\nmay be\nexpanded to embrace methods for measuring and/or\nanalyzing some of these variables,\nbut at present\nthey are not included.\nSome of the more important\nmeasurements\nof this class are briefly described\nin\nthis section, but none of them are discussed\nin\ndetail.\n\nCloud cover (expressed\nas fractional coverage\nin octals, or percent) is essential metadata used for\nassessing\ndata quality and screening questionable\ncases from algorithm development\nand validation\nanalyses.\nA description\nof sky conditions near the\nsun and satellite\nzenith\nand azimuth\nangles,\nincluding\nwhether\nthe sun is obscured\nduring\nobservations,\nis also important information.\nCloud\ntype information\nis also useful, as are photographs\nof sky conditions.\nSecchi depth measurements\nare required\nfor\nreal-time assessment\nof water transparency\nduring a\nstation and as a quality check during analysis of\nradiometric\nprofiles.\nWater depth, in m, is important information for\nscreening\ndata from shallow\nwater cases where\nbottom reflections\nmay be present in water-leaving\nradiance measurements.\nHydrographic\ndata, water temperature (T), and\nsalinity (S), derived from conductivity,\ntemperature,\nand\ndepth\n(CTD)\nprofiles,\nare\nuseful\nfor\ncharacterizing\nthe physical water mass regime in\nwhich\nan optical\nprofile\nis measured.\nA T-S\ncharacterization\nis especially\nimportant near ocean\nfronts and eddies where interleaving\nwater masses\n\nAerosol\nconcentration\nsamples\nusing high\nvolume techniques\nwill be useful, in conjunction\nwith aerosol optical depth spectra determined\nfrom\nsun photometer\nmeasurements,\nfor chemical,\nsize,\nand\nabsorption\ncharacterization\nof\naerosols,\nespecially\nin studies of the effects of Saharan and\nAsian dust clouds on atmospheric corrections.\nParticulates,\nboth POC and PON, are required\nfor process studies to help characterize\nthe adaptive\nstate of phytoplankton\nand to inventory\ncritical\nbiogeochemical\nelements.\nDOC has been shown to be a major pool of\ncarbon\nin the\noceans.\nQuantification\nof the\ntransformations\nof\nthis\npool\nis crucial\nto\nunderstanding\nthe marine carbon cycle. The colored\nfraction, CDOM, of the DOC is highly absorbent in\nthe blue range, thus decreasing\nblue water-leaving\nradiances, and it must be taken into consideration\n\nof very different biogeochemical\ncomposition,\nand\ntherefore\nfundamentally\ndifferent\nbio-optical\nproperties,\ncan\nproduce\ncomplex\nspatial\nand\ntemporal patterns of near-surface optical properties.\nIn these circumstances,\nT-S profiles can provide.an\nindication of whether a station location is suitable\nfor reliable remote sensing validation and algorithm\ndevelopment\ncomparisons.\nThe T(z) and S(z)\nmeasurements\nare also needed for corrections\nto\npure\nwater\nmeasurements.\n\nabsorption\n\nin\n\nprocessing\n\nMODEL\nDATA\n\nfor\npigment\nconcentration\nalgorithms.\nDOC\nmeasurements\nare\nneeded\nto develop\nrobust\nrelationships\nbetween CDOM and DOC, which are\nneeded to evaluate the usefulness\nof ocean color\nobservations\nfor estimating DOC concentrations.\nCDOM concentrations\n,are required\nto assess\nthe effect of Gelbstoff\non blue water-leaving\nradiances and chlorophyll\nconcentration.\nThis is of\nprimary importance\nin Case-2 waters, but is also\nrelevant to phytoplankton\ndegradation\nproducts\nin\nCase-2 waters.\n\nIOP\n\n12\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor\nO\nC Sensor\nValidation\nHumic and fulvic\nCDOM\nand\nhave\n\nBalch, W.M., P.M. Mueller\n\nSensing,\n\n3.1 INTRODUCTION\nA central focus of the SIMBIOS program, and\nof independent\nvalidation activities in the SeaWiFS\nand other ocean\ncolor\nsensor projects,\nis the\nestimation\nof\nuncertainties\nin\nsatellite\ndeterminations\nof\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance\n(or equivalently,\nnormalized\nremotesensing\nreflectance),\natmospheric\ncorrection\nand\nbio-optical\nalgorithms,\nand derived products.\nIn\nmost cases, statistical\ncomparisons\nwith in situ\nmeasurements\n- or quantities derived from in situ\nmeasurements\n- play a central role in estimating the\nuncertainties\nin\nthe\nsatellite\nocean\ncolor\nmeasurements,\nalgorithms\nand derived\nproducts.\nThe uncertainty\nbudgets of in situ measurements\nused for comparisons\nare obvious critical factors in\nsuch validation\nanalyses,\nas also are details and\nuncertainties\nof critical\ndesign and performance\ncharacteristics\nof the instruments\nwith which they\nare measured.\nThis and the next several\nchapters\nspecify\n\nSan\n\nDiego\n\nState\n\nUniversity,\n\nCalifornia\n\ndocument\ndoes not provide detailed methods for\ncalibrating\nmeteorological\nsensors,\nCTD\ninstruments,\npressure\ntransducers,\nand\nother\nancillary\nsensors.\nSections\n3.8 through\n3.10\nemphasize\nthe\nimportance\nof using\nproperly\ncalibrated\nsensors\nto make\nthese\nimportant\nsupporting\nmeasurements,\nbut a well-established\ninfrastructure\nfor these calibration\nservices exists\nwithin the general\ncommunities.\n\noceanographic\n\nand atmospheric\n\n3.2 EXTRATERRESTRIAL\nSOLAR FLUX SPECTRUM\nThese protocols,\nand SeaWiFS,\nMODIS\nand\nCZCS algorithms,\nare all predicated\non using a\nsingle\ndetermination\nof\nthe\nspectrum\nof\nextraterrestrial\nsolar irradiance\nfor the average\ndistance\n\nbetween\n\nthe earth and sun, /_o(_).\n\nWithin\n\nthe ocean color remote sensing and ocean optics\ncommunities,\nthe presently accepted extraterrestrial\nsolar flux spectrum\nis that of Neckel\nand Labs\n(1984). There is less unanimity in the atmospheric\ncommunity,\nand\nin some\nsegments\nof the\ninternational\nremote sensing\ncommunity,\nin the\nchoice of a "standard" solar spectrum.\nIt is absolutely essential that a single, common\nstandard\nsolar flux spectrum\nbe used in every\naspect of research and validation\nin ocean co_lor\nremote sensing.\nThe extraterrestrial\nsolar flux\nenters into normalization\nof water leaving radiance,\ncalibration\nand\ninterpretation\nof\natmospheric\nradiation\nmeasurements,\nand\natmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms\nfor all satellite ocean color\nradiometers.\nFor example,\nif normalized\nwater\nleaving\nradiance\nwere computed\nfrom in situ\nmeasurements\nusing a "better" estimate of the solar\nflux, in lieu of Neckei\nand Labs\n(1984),\na\ncomparison\nwith a satellite\ndetermination\nof\nnormalized\nwater-leaving\nradiance would be biased\nby the difference\nbetween\nthe two solar spectra.\nThere is some evidence (Biggar 1998; Schmid et al.\n1998) that the recent measurements\nof Thuillier et\n\nappropriate\ninstrument characteristics\nand describe\naccepted\nlaboratory\nprocedures\nfor characterizing\ninstruments\nto\ndetermine\nand\nverify\ntheir\ncompliance\nwith those\nspecifications.\nDetailed\ncharacterization\nand\ncalibration\nprotocols\nfor\nradiometers\nand sun photometers\nare provided\nin\nChapters 5, 6 and 7. The status of each of these\nchapters,\nand topic areas in each where future\nadvances\nand/or changes may be appropriate,\nare\ndiscussed\nbriefly\nin sections\n3.3 through\n3.6.\nBecause of the time constraints\nfor publication\nof\nthis version of the Ocean Optics Protocols,\nit has\nnot been possible\nto provide\na similar in-depth\ntreatment\nfor characterization\nand calibration\nof\ninstruments\nused\nto measure\ninherent\noptical\nproperties\nregarding\nabstracted\n\nand\n\n(IOP).\nThe current\nstate of the art\nIOP instrument\ncalibration\nis briefly\nbelow in Section\n3.7. The SIMBIOS\n\nProject Office plans to sponsor focused workshops\nto prepare\nprotocol chapters\non IOP topics for a\nfuture update of the Ocean Optics Protocols.\nThis\n\n17\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor ensor\nO\nC S\nValidation\nal. (1998)\n\nare more consistent\n\nwith NIST\n\ntraceable\n\n2.\n\nlamp-based\nirradiance and radiance sources. On the\nbasis of such findings, it seems clear that NASA\nand the international\nocean color community should\nreconsider\nthe\nchoice\nof\na\nstandard\nfor\nextraterrestrial\nsolar flux. Assuming that a change\nwould\nimprove\nthe uncertainty\nbudget\nof, e.g.\natmospheric\ncorrection\nvalidations,\nthe expected\nbenefits are obvious.\nOn the other hand, adopting a\ndifferent\nsolar spectrum would require significant\nchanges\nin the software\nused\nfor operational\nprocessing\nand\nvalidation\nanalyses\nwithin\nSeaWiFS,\nMODIS and other ocean color satellite\nproject\noffices.\nAny such transition\nmust be\nplanned\nand implemented\ncomprehensively\nin a\nforum\nthat\nembraces\nthe entire\ninternational\n\n3.4 CHARACTERIZATION\nOF\nOCEANOGRAPHIC\nAND\nATMOSPHERIC\nRADIOMETERS\nThe procedures given here are essentially those\nfrom Mueller\nand Austin (1995).\nChanges\nand\nadditions\nprimarily\nreflect\nresults\nand lessons\nlearned from the SeaWiFS Intercomparison\nRoundRobin Experiment\n(SIRREX) series (e.g. Mueller et\nal. 1996; Johnson et al. 1996) and deal primarily\nwith methods for transferring\nthe NIST scale of\nspectral irradiance from an FEL lamp source to the\nresponsivity\nscales\nof\noceanographic\nand\natmospheric\nradiometers.\nThe SIMBIOS\nand\nSeaWiFS\nProject\nOffices\nare\ncontinuing\nthe\nSIRREX series to a_ssure maintenance\nof consistent\nradiometric\ncalibration uncertainties\nthroughout\nthe\ncommunity\n(Riley and Bailey 199g)and\nfor be[ter\ndetermination\nof, e.g., quantitative\nuncertaintites\nassociated\nwith\nradiance\ncalibrations\nusing\nSpectralon plaques (S. Hooker, pers. comm.).\nThe Chapter\n5 protocols\nhave also\nbeen\nchanged to recommend\nexperimental\ndetermination\nof\nimmersion\nfactors\nfor\nevery\nindividual\nunderwater\nirradiance\ncollector.\nIn Mueller\nand\nAustin (1995),\nit was suggested\nthat immersion\nfactors\ndetermined\nfor a prototype\nirradiance\ncollector could be used for other radiance collectors\n\ncommunity.\nIt would be appropriate\nfor this task to\nbe addressed\nby a working group convened\nunder\nthe auspices\nof the International\nOcean\nColor\nCoordinating\nGroup (IOCCG).\nUntil such time as a new standard is adopted,\nhowever, compliance\nwith the present Ocean Optics\nProtocols requires that any analysis, or application,\ninvolving\n\nextraterrestrial\n\nuse the scale of Neckel\n\nsolar\n\nirradiance\n\nFo(2)\n\nand Labs (1984).\n\n3.3 INSTRUMENT\nPERFORMANCE\nSPECIFICATIONS\nChapter 4 provides detailed specifications\nfor\nperformance-related\ncharacteristics\nof radiometers,\nand other types of instruments, that measure in situ\nvariables\nused to validate\nsatellite\nocean color\n\nof the same size, design and material specifications.\nThe results of Mueller (1995) demonstrated\nthat\nindividual\ndeviations\nbetween\ncollectors\nof the\nsame design, size and materials may be as large as\n8%, with a 3% RMS uncertainty\nfor the group of\nsuch instruments tested. several\ncomponents\n\nmeteorological\nof uncertainty\n\nbudgets associated with using in situ measurements\nto validate satellite ocean color measurements\nand\nalgorithms.\nUncertainty\nin barometric pressure can\naffect that of absorption\nterms in atmospheric\ncorrection\nalgorithms.\nUncertainty\nin surface wind\nvelocity directly affects sun and sky glint reflection\nestimates\nused to correct water-leaving\nradiance\ndeterminations\nfrom satellites\nand in situ abovewater\nradiometers.\nAnemometers,\nbarometers,\n\ncoefficients\nK(z,L).\nIf an instrument\'s\npressure\ntransducer port is equipped with a threaded fitting,\na hose filled with distilled water may be used to\nconnect it to a hand-pump and NIST traceable dead\nweight tester (several\nmodels\nare commercially\navailable).\nAnother common\narrangement\nis to\nimmerse\nthe instrument\nin a pressure\nchamber,\nwhich is connected in turn to the pump and pressure\ncalibration\ndevice. In either case, water pressure is\nincreased\nin steps to produce\nseveral\nreadings\nspanning\nthe operating\nrange of the instrument\nunder test, and a polynomial\nequation is fit to the\ndata to relate transducer\noutput to the pressures\nmeasured\nwith the dead-weight\ntester.\nDetailed\nmethods\nand a certificate\nof NIST\ntraceable\ncalibration\nshould\nbe\nobtained\nfrom\nthe\nmanufacturer\nof the pressure\ncalibration\ndevice.\nCalibration\nservices\nof this type\nare readily\navailable, on a fee-for-service\nbasis, at laboratories\nmaintained by many oceanography\ndepartments\nand\ncommercial\nvendors of oceanographic\nequipment.\nIf simultaneous\ndeployment\nof the CTD with\noptical\ninstruments\nhaving\nindependent\npressure\ntransducers is practical, the two depths measured by\nthe different instruments\nshould be compared\nover\nthe range of the cast. If depth\nmeasurements\ndisagree\nsignificantly,\nthese comparisons\nmay be\nused to correct whichever transducer\nis found to be\n\nthermometers\n(air temperature),\nand hygrometers\nshould be calibrated using methods and at intervals\nrecommended\nby\nthe\nWorld\nMetorological\nOrganization\n(WMO).\nCalibration\nservices\nand\ncertification\nare available through the vendors who\nsupply\nmeteorological\ninstruments,\nand in the\nlaboratories\nof some\nacademic\noceanographic\nand/or atmospheric\ninstitutions.\n\n3.9 CTD CALIBRATION\nThe conductivity\nprobe, temperature probe, and\npressure\ntransducer\nof the CTD\nshould\nbe\nrecalibrated\nbefore and after each major cruise by a\nproperly\nequipped\nphysical\noceanographic\nlaboratory,\nincluding\nthose maintained\nby many\nuniversity\noceanography\ndepartments\nand CTD\nmanufacturers.\nIn addition, the conductivity\nprobe\nshould\nbe independently\ncalibrated\nduring\nthe\ncourse of each cruise by obtaining\nsalinity water\nsamples\nsimultaneous\nwith CTD readings. These\nsalinity samples are to be analyzed, either at sea or\nashore,\nwith a laboratory\nsalinometer\ncalibrated\nwith International\nAssociation\nfor the Physical\nSciences of the Ocean (IAPSO) Standard Seawater.\n\n3.10\n\nPRESSURE\n\nin error through analysis\nof prepressure transducer calibrations.\n\nand post-cruise\n\nREFERENCES\nBalch, W.M.,\nD.T. Mueller\nRemote\n\nand\n\nRoswell\n\nSensing,\n\n4.1INTRODUCTION\nThis report describes\nmeasurements\nof optical\nproperties,\nand other\nvariables,\nnecessary\nfor\nvalidating data obtained with satellite ocean color\ninstruments,\nand for the development\nof in-water\nand atmospheric\nalgorithms.\nThe specifications\nherein are those required\nof instruments\nused on\nships, or other platforms,\nto acquire that in situ\ndata. In some cases, the specifications\nhave been\nselected\nto allow\nuse of instruments\nthat are\naffordable and that either currently exist, or that can\nbe developed\nwithout\nmajor\nimprovements\nin\ntoday\'s state-of-the-art\ntechnology.\nIn a few cases,\nnew or improved instruments must be developed to\nrealize the specified\nperformance\ncharacteristics.\nThe data uncertainty\nrequirements\nfor this program\nare more severe than those for a general ocean\nsurvey.\nHere,\nvarious\ninvestigators\nwill use a\nvariety\nof instruments\nthat will be calibrated\nindependently\nat a number\nof facilities,\nand\ncontribute data to a common database which will be\nused to validate SeaWiFS and other satellite ocean\ncolor measurements.\nThe resulting radiometrie\nand\n\nSan\n\nSpectral\n\nmeasure\n\nState\n\nUniversity,\n\nCalifornia\n\nCharacteristics\n\ndatabase, the wavelength combinations\nin Table 4-1\nmust\nbe expanded\nto provide\nradiance\nand\nirradiance\nmeasurements\nat the greater number of\nwavelengths\nrepresented\nby the full ensemble\nof\nocean color sensors (Appendix\nA). Sampling over longer periods of time\nof\nr\nto\nexact\ncenter\nwavelength\nand detailed\nspectral\nresponse\nfunction\n(Sections\n4.1.1 and 5.1.2), any\nuse of airborne\nradiometers\nmust quantitatively\naccount\nfor the different\nspectral\nresponsivity\nfunctions\nbetween\nmeasurements\nof radiance\nby,\ne.g., SeaWiFS,\nin-water\nradiometers,\nand abovewater radiometers\nat each channel\'s nominal center\n\nmay be done by either electronic\nintegration\nof all\nchannels simultaneously,\nor by averaging\nmultiple\nscans.\n\nA radiometer\'s\nsensitivity to the polarization\nof\naperture radiance is critical for ocean color remote\nsensing\napplications.\nPolarization\nsensitivity\nis\nlikely to be present\nin any radiometer\nhaving\nmirrors, prisms or gratings in its optical path. To\nmeasure\naccurate\nwater-leaving\nradiances\nusing\ninstruments\nof these types,\nit is necessary\nto\ndepolarize\naperture\nradiance\nusing either fiberoptics or a pseudo-depolarizer.\nShipboard\nand\nairborne\nocean color radiometers\nmust have a\npolarization\nsensitivity\nof less than 2% in all\nchannels. The sole exception to this rule will occur\nin the case of instruments\ndesigned\nto actually\nmeasure the polarization\ncomponents\nof aperture\nradiance,\ne.g., the polarization\nchannels\nof the\nFrench\nPolarization\nand Directionality\nof the\nEarth\'s Reflectances\n(POLDER)\ninstrument\nand of\nthe band-held SIMBAD radiometers.\n\nwavelength.\nA high-altitude\nimaging radiometer\nmust have\na radiometric\nuncertainty\nand SNR in all channels\nequal\nto those\nof the satellite\nocean\ncolor\ninstrument\nif its imagery is to be used for direct\nradiometric\nverification\nof the satellite\nsensor\'s\nradiometric\nperformance.\nIn some\ncases,\nthe\nrequisite\nSNR may be realized\nthrough\npixel\naveraging\nto\na\n1km\nspatial\nresolution\ncommensurate\nwith that of, e.g., SeaWiFS.\nDirect\nradiometric\ncomparisons\nbetween\naircraft\nand\nSeaWiFS radiances,\nhowever, also require that the\ndifferent\natmospheric\npath effects\nbe carefully\nmodeled, and that the uncertainty in those modeled\nadjustments\nbe independently\nestimated.\nThis can\nbe done\nmost\neffectively\nwhen\nthe\naircraft\nmeasurements\nare combined\nwith the full suite of\n\nEach application\nof a particular\nabove-water\nradiometer\nsystem, if it is proposed\nfor satellite\nocean color sensor validation, must be evaluated on\nits own merits.\nThe\ninstrument\'s\nresponsivity,\nuncertainty,\nstability,\nFOV,\nand\nspectral\ncharacteristics\nmust be evaluated\nin the context of\n\nshipboard\nin-water,\natmospheric,\nand ancillary\nmeasurements\n(Table\n2-1). In this case, direct\ncomparisons\nbetween aircraft and ship radiometry\nmay require that both the SNR and the uncertainties\nrealized in combined\nanalyses of the two data sets\nwill represent\na smaller spatial resolution than the\nnominal 1 km instantaneous\nfield-of-view\n(IFOV)\nfor SeaWiFS.\nFinally,\nthe viewing\nzenith and\nazimuth angles at the matched pixel must also be\nnearly the same for both sensors, if uncertainties\nassociated\nwith modeled corrections\nfor the ocean\'s\nsurface\nand\ninternal\nbi-directional\nreflectance\n\nthe models to be used to compare\nits radiance\nmeasurements\nto in-water, or SeaWiFS,\nradiance\nmeasurements.\nThe suitability of spatial averaging\nto improve SNRs must be evaluated in terms of the\nspatial variability prevailing in the experiment\nsite,\nparticularly\nwhen in-water and aircraft radiances\nare to be directly\ncompared.\nFiner\nresolution\naircraft imagery, or low-altitude\ntrackline data, will\noften be essential for determining\nthe validity of\nattempts\nto directly compare\nin-water\nand, e.g.,\nSeaWiFS radiances measured at a particular site.\nIn summary,\nairborne\nand shipboard\nabovewater\nradiometry\ncan\nobviously\ncontribute\nextremely\nvaluable\ndata\nfor\nvalidating\nthe\nradiometric\nperformance\nof satellite\nocean color\ninstruments and the algorithms employed with their\ndata. There is, however, a wide possible range of\nradiometer characteristics\nthat can be applied to this\nprogram,\nand detailed\nspecification\nof required\ncharacteristics\ncan only be done in the context of\neach\nparticular\nexperiment\'s\ndesign.\nOnly\nthe\n\ndistribution\nfunction (BRDF) are to be avoided.\nPerformance\ncharacteristic\nspecifications\nare\nsimilar for ocean color radiometers\nused to measure\nwater-leaving\nradiance\nfrom either the deck of a\nship or an aircraft flown at low altitude, i.e., 200m\naltitude\nor\nlower.\nRadiometric\ncharacteristics\nshould match the criterion set forth for in-water L_\nQ.) radiometers\nin Sections 4.1.1--4.1.4\nand Tables\n4-1 through 4-3. The instrument\nFOV should be\nbetween 5\xc2\xb0and 10 \xc2\xb0 (full angle), and all wavelengths\nmust be coregistered\nwithin 10% of the IFOV. The response function thus measured is\nthen normalized\nto the maximum (peak).\nAlthough the results of this characterization\nare\nusually\nrepresented\nby\nonly\nthe\nnominal\nwavelength\nand\nFWHM\nbandpass,\nthe\nfull\nnormalized\nresponse\nfunction should be recorded\nfor use in detailed\nwavelength\nadjustments\nand\ncomparisons\nwith the SeaWiFS\nand other sensor\nchannel\nresponse\nfunctions,\nwhich will not be\nknown until shortly before launch. It is further\nrecommended\nthat\nthe\ninternal\ninstrument\n\nSTANDARDS\n\nThe portable irradiance and radiance reference\nstandard to be used to trace instrument stability\nduring field deployments\n(Chapter\n7) should be\nplaced\nin position\non the sensor immediately\nfollowing the calibration to establish the instrument\nresponse to this reference unit.\nBetween\nradiometric\ncalibration\nactivities,\nstable lamp sources\nin rugged,\nfixed\nconfigurations\nshould be used to track\nperformance.\nIrradiance channels can be\nwith irradiance\nsources at fixed distances\ncollectors, while radiance sources can be\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\ngeometric\ninstrument\nmonitored\nfrom the\nmonitored\n\nby filling the FOV with diffuser plates placed in\nfront\nof the irradiance\nsources,\nor by using\nintegrating\ncavity sources.\nIn each case, careful\nattention\nmust\nbe\ngiven\nto fixing\nspecific\ngeometries\nof source and detector in each use. The\nstability of the lamp output and the repeatability\nof\nmeasurement\nmust be sufficient\nto detect\n2%\nvariations\nin an instrument\'s\nperformance.\nAn\ninstrument\nshould be connected\nto the portable\nstandard and its response recorded daily, keeping a\nrecord\nof instrument\nresponsivity\nthroughout\nan\nexperiment.\nFurthermore,\nthese\nsources\nwould\nprovide an essential warning of problems\nif they\nappear. The portable field reference source must be\navailable\nwhen\nthe\ncomplete\nradiometric\ncalibrations\nare performed so that a baseline may be\nestablished\nand maintained\nfor each sensor channel\n(Section 4.1.1). Each underwater\ninstrument\nmust be individually\ncharacterized\nover the range of\n-2--400 C. In the case of deck cells, the temperature\nrange for testing should be extended to 10----450 C.\nSensors exhibiting\ntemperature\ncoefficients\ngreater\nthan 0.01% per \xc2\xb0C over this temperature\nrange,\nshould be fully characterized\nover their respective\nranges to establish\nthe means and precision\nwith\nwhich post-acquisition\nprocessing\ncan be used to\ncorrect\nfor temperature\ndependency.\nAlthough\nknowledge\nof the zero, or dark current, drift is\nessential\nfor working at the lowest radiances\nor\nirradiances,\nit should be emphasized\nthat more\nsignificant\nnear-surface\nerrors may be induced by\ntemperature\nvariations in responsivity.\nThese possible responsivity\nchanges must be\nindividually\ndetermined\nacross the spectrum. In the\nabove\ndiscussion,\nthe temperatures\ncited\nare\nenvironmental\ntemperatures,\nbut it should\nbe\nemphasized\nthat any correction\nmust\nuse the\ntemperature\nof the affected\nelement,\nwhich is\nnormally\nin the interior of the instrument.\nThis is\nbest accomplished\nby routinely\nusing temperature\nsensors\nplaced\nat critical\nlocations\nwithin the\ninstrument.\nFor\nhighest\nprecision,\ndynamic\ntemperature\ntesting involving\ntemporal\ntransients,\nas well as possible temperature gradients within an\ninstrument, may be appropriate.\n\n5.10 PRESSURE\nPressure\n\ncan cause\n\ndefined;\n\nnew\n\nTRANSDUCER\n\nCALIBRATION\nThe radiometer\'s pressure transducer,\nwhich is\nused to measure instrument depth during profiles,\nshould be tested and calibrated\nbefore and after\neach major cruise.\n\n5.12 POLARIZATION\nSENSITIVITY\nPolarization\nsensitivity\nis more\ncritical\nin\nabove-water\nradiometry\nthan\nunderwater\nradiometry.\nIf a radiometer\nmeasures\npolarization\ncomponents\nof radiance, then its responsivity\nand\nrejection\nof cross-polarization\nradiance\nmust be\ncharacterized\nfor each component\nchannel.\nFor\nabove-water\nscalar radiance\ninstruments,\nas with\nthe SeaWiFS\nand other ocean color radiometers,\nsensitivity\nto linear polarization\nmust be less than\n2%, and the actual degree of polarization\nsensitivity\nmust be characterized\nfor each channel.\nA generalized\nprotocol for characterizing\nthe\npolarization\nsensitivity\nof a radiometer\nis given\nhere. Some photometers are\nmanually aimed at the sun using sun targeting\nmechanisms.\nMicroTops\nII (Morays et al., 1998,\nPorter et al, 1999) and SIMBAD (Deschamps et al.,\n2000,\nFougnie\net al., 1999a,\n1999b) are two\nexamples of hand-held\nsun photometers. Hence, the accuracy greatly depends on\nthe\ngeographical\nlocation\nof the\ncalibration\nexperiment.\nThe calibration\nis generally performed\nin conditions where the stability of the atmosphere\nand a low aerosol contribution\nenable high accuracy\nof the method (Holben et al. 1998, Schmid et al.\n1998). Since\nVoltz, 1959, several papers have discussed different\nmethods to improve the solar calibration. Schmid et\nal., 1998, used lamp and solar calibrations\nin\nconjunction\nwith each other. O\'Neill et al., 1984,\ncombined\nsolar aureole and solar beam extinction.\n\n(Vermeulen\net al., 2000). This section is dedicated\nto the description\nof calibration\ntechniques\nfor\naccurate retrievals of sky radiances.\n\nSoufflet et al., 1992, and Holben et al., 1998, used a\nwell-calibrated\nsun photometer as a reference.\nThe degradation\nof interference\nfilters is the\nmost important source of the long-term variability\nin\nthe\ncross\ncalibration.\nAlthough\nmajor\nimprovements\nhave been made on the filter design\n(interference\nfilters, ion-assisted\ndeposition),\nthe\nfilters remain the main factor limiting performance\nof\nsun\nphotometers.\nDegradation\nof\nfilters\nnecessitates\nfrequent\ncalibration\nof\nsun\nphotometers,\nand\nmeasurements\nof the filter\ntransmission\nor the\nrelative\nsystem\nresponse\n(Schmid et al., 1998). The degradation\nof the filters\nmounted on the CIMEL sun photometers\nhas been\nmonitored\nsince 1993. Degradation\nreported\nby\nHolben\net al., 1998, within the first 2 years of\nCIMEL operation is between 1 and 5%.\n\nPREDE, are calibrated using an integrating\nsphere.\nThe radiometer\nis aligned in front of the sphere\n(Figure 6.3, top) and I0 measurements\nare taken for\neach channel. A 100%\n\nGSFC. A Lambertian\nsource is necessary to provide\nthe input light to the box, therefore, an integrating\nsphere is usually used. POLBOX\nis composed\nof\ntwo adjustable\nglass blades\nwhich have a high\nrefractive\nindex. The blades are placed in a black\nanodized\naluminum\nalloy box. These portable standards are an important\npart of the recommended\ninstrument package.\n\nthe\n\nSQM\n\nAlthough Mueller\nneed\nfor,\nand\n\nand Austin\ndescribed\n\n(1995)\nsome\n\nGreenbelt,\n\nMaryland\n\ngiven\nhere.\nA\nseparate\nrack\nof electronic\nequipment, composed principally of two computercontrolled power supplies and a multiplexed,\ndigital\nvoltmeter\n(DVM),\nare\nan essential\npart\nof\nproducing the stable light field. All of the external\ncomponents\nare controlled\nby a computer program\nover a general purpose interface bus (GPIB).\nThe SQM has two sets of halogen lamps with\neight lamps in each set; both lamp sets are arranged\nsymmetrically\non a ring and operate in series, so if\none lamp fails, the entire set goes off. The lamps in\none set are rated for 1.05 A (4.2 V) and are\noperated at 0.95 A, and the lamps in the other set\nare rated for 3.45 A (5.0 V) and are operated at 3.1\nA; the lamp sets are hereafter referred to as the 1 A\nand 3 A lamps,\nrespectively.\nThe lamps\nare\noperated\nat approximately\n95 % of their full\namperage\nrating to maximize\nthe lifetime\nof the\nlamps.\nA low, medium, and high intensity flux level is\nprovided when the 1A, 3A, and both lamp sets are\nused, respectively.\nEach lamp set was aged for\napproximately\n50 hours before deploying\nthe SQM\nto the field. The interior light chamber has beadblasted aluminum walls, so the diffuse component\nof the reflectance\nis significant.\nThe\nlamps\nilluminate\na circular plastic diffuser protected\nby\nsafety glass and sealed from the environment\nby orings.\nThe diffuser\nis resilient\nto ultraviolet\nyellowing,\nbut can age nonetheless.\nThe exit\naperture is 20 cm in diameter\nand has a spatial\nuniformity of 98 % or more over the interior 15 cm\ncircle. The SQM does not have, nor does it require,\nan absolute calibration,\nbut it has design objectives\nof\nbetter\nthan\n2 %\nstability\nduring\nfield\ndeployments.\nA faceplate\nor shadow\ncollar\nprovides\na\nmounting\nassembly,\nso the device\nunder\ntest\n(DUT), usually a radiance or irradiance\nsensor, can\nbe positioned in the shadow collar. The DUT has a\nD-shaped collar fitted to it at a set distance, 3.81 cm\n(1.5 inch), from the front of the DUT. The entire system consists\nof two components,\na deck box that provides DC\npower\nto the SQM-II,\nand the SQM-II\nitself\n(McLean et al. 1998). The latter contains the lamp\nrings (which use the same lamps as the original\nSQM),\nheating and cooling\nsubsystems,\ncontrol\ncircuitry,\nthe system computer,\nplus display and\ndata storage. The SQM-II system is designed to be\nself contained and does not require a computer to\noperate. Only two cables are required to complete\nsystem assembly (an AC power cord for the deck\nbox and a DC power cord to link the deck box to\nthe SQM-II).\nAlthough\nthis integration\nreduces\nsystem\ncomplexity,\nit comes\nwith\nincreased\nvulnerability:\na failure in any one of the subsystems\ncan render the entire system inoperable\nwith no\nopportunity\nfor simply\nswapping\nin a new\n(external)\nsubassembly,\nlike a power\nsupply or\nDVM.\nAs was done with the original\nSQM,\nSatlantic\nrecommends\nrunning the SQM-II on an\nuninterruptable\npower supply (UPS).\nUser input to start and monitor the system is\nvia a simple 4-button\nkeypad\nand a 4 x 20\nfluorescent\ndisplay\nat the rear of the device.\nCommands\ncan be entered using the menus on the\ndisplay or remotely from a computer.\nA computer\ncan also be connected\nto the system to log data\nduring a measurement\nsession, or the data can be\nstored internally\nin a flash card and downloaded\nlater.\n\nwithout\n\nAn\ninternal\nthermally\nstabilized\ncurrent\nregulation\ncircuit ensures precise current regulation\nto the two independent\nlamp sets (with low- and\nhigh-power\nlamps).\nThe lamps\nin the original\ndesign were potted into aluminum\nmounts which\nheld the bulbs in their correct orientations.\nThe\nmounts were soldered to a circular circuit board and\nwere\ndifficult\nto replace.\nIn the OCS-5002,\nporcelain sockets are used for each lamp, which are\nheld in place with epoxy in aluminum\nmounting\nrings. This design allows for rapid individual bulb\nreplacement.\nShunt temperatures\nas well as the lamp housing\ntemperatures\nare monitored\nduring operation.\nA\ntwo-channel\nfilter-detector\nand a third unfiltered\n\n......\n\ndetector are positioned within the lamp housing to\npermit direct optical monitoring\nof the lamp rings\nand the integrating\ncavity\nitself.\nThese\nthree\n\nFigure 7.3.\nDUT mounted\n\ndetectors\nare\nthermally\nstabilized\nvia\na\nthermoelectrically\ncooled housing to approximately\n35\xc2\xb0C, and their outputs are continuously\nmonitored\nduring system operation.\n\nThe differences\nbetween the two SQM units\nare not restricted to their control architecture.\nThe\nSQM-II has many improvements\nthat use of the\n\n59\n\nA picture of the SQM-II\nto the shadow collar.\n\nwith a\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean Sensor\nO Color\nValidation\noriginalunit has shownto be desirable nder\nu\ndifferent\ncircumstances:\n1. In its simplest form, a\nCERT session is a sequence of DAS events which\nare executed\nfollowing a prescribed\nmethodology.\nEach DAS represents\nenough data to statistically\nestablish\nthe characteristics\nof the instrument\ninvolved within a reasonable\namount of time. approach for presenting the data analysis\nprocedures is assumed to involve more than one\nradiometer,\nsince most deployment systems involve\na solar reference\nand one or more above- or inwater instruments. In the most general terms, the\nquantity of interest is a voltage or digital count\nlevel associated with a radiometer (or DUT), VC(_,\ntj), where V is the voltage of the radiometer\nunder\nillumination at the time of the measurement, C is\n\n-_ 1 "W\nv,(t,)---E (z,,t,)\nn ,/-1\n\nthe instrument code of the DUT, Li is an individual\nwavelength or channel of the instrument, and tj is a\nparticular time for the data record. The instrument\ncode is just a simple mnemonic for keeping track of\nwhich DUT was measured when. A suitable coding\nscheme is to assign a letter for a particular type of\nradiometer (e.g., R for radiance, I for irradiance,\netc.) and then to add on the serial number).\nAn SQM has two lamp sets, so multiple flux\nlevels are possible. concentration\nof\n\nIn addition\nto determining\nthe bio-optical\ncategory and characteristics\nof a particular\nwater\nmass, the validation\nsampling\nstrategy\nmust be\nconcerned\nwith spatial and temporal\nvariability.\nSpatial\nand temporal\nvariability\nin bio-optical\nproperties\nwill profoundly\naffect the validity\nof\ncomparisons\nbetween satellite and in-water optical\nmeasurements.\nA single SeaWiFS\ninstantaneous\nFOV measurement,\nfor example,\nwill integrate\nLw(L) over approximately\na square kilometer, or a\n\noften\nbeen\nloosely\nlumped\ninto the\nCase-2\ndefinition,\nalbeit with considerable\nconfusion over\n\nlarger area at viewing\nangles away from nadir.\nFurthermore,\nthe location uncertainty\nfor a single\npixel may be several kilometers,\nexcept in is\nonly a small fraction of a kilometer.\nData from a\ngr!d of several station locations may be required to\nestimate the spatial averages of optical properties\nrepresented\nby a satellite pixel, or a block of pixels.\nBecause the ship measurements\nover the grid are\nnot instantaneous,\ntemporal\nvariability\nin biooptical properties can add additional\nuncertainty\nto\nthe comparisons.\nAircraft radiometric\nobservations\ncan,\nconceptually,\nbe used\nboth\nto locate\ncomparison sites away from areas of strong spatial\nvariability and to document changes in the pattern\nof spatial variability over the period required for a\nship to occupy all stations in a comparison\ngrid.\nVertical\nstratification\nof water temperature,\nsalinity,\nand density\noften affect\nthe vertical\nstructure\nof variability\nin bio-optical\nproperties.\nThis variability,\nin turn, affects the remote sensing\nreflectance.\nVertical\nstratification\nof the water\n\nhow to Most areas in\nthe deep ocean belong to this case. Water masses\nthat do not satisfy these criteria will be grouped\nunder the heading Case-2. Within Case-2, by this\ndefinition,\nwater masses with a wide diversity\nof\nbio-optical\ncharacteristics\nwill be found.
