may\ninclude colored\nterrigenous\nconstituents\nsuch as\n\n68\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor ensor\nO\nC S\nValidation\nCDOM\nandsuspended\nsediments.these\nIn\nCase-2\n\nblooms\n\nwaters,\ndown\n\nconcentrations\nare elevated by the influx of river\nwater.\nIn these circumstances,\nit is especially\ncritical to avoid disturbing the vertical stratification\nof the water column with the ship\'s propellers.\n\nthe global color\nbecause\ntwo or\n\nratio\nmore\n\nalgorithms\nsubstances\n\nbreak\nwith\n\ndifferent optical properties are present which do not\nco-vary\nwith chlorophyll\na concentration.\nThese\nmight be waters with exceptional\nplankton blooms\n(such as red tides),\nareas discolored\nby dust\ntransported\nby the wind from deserts into the sea, or\ncoastal\nareas\ninfluenced\nby river discharge\nof\nmineral\nand organic\nsuspended\nmaterials,\nand\nCDOM, i.e. gelbstoffe, such as humic acids.\nIt is not always easy to decide to which case a\nwater mass belongs. As a starting point, the water\nbelongs to Case-2 if any of the four Case-! criteria,\nset forth above, are not satisfied. For Case-2 waters\ndefined by any one of these criteria, it remains a\nfurther\nproblem\nto determine\nthe specific\nbiooptical characteristics\nthat distinguish it from Case1. Case-2 sampling must usually include both the\n"Required"\nand "Highly\nDesired"\nvariables,\nas\nidentified in Table 2.1, plus SPM. For example, it\nmay be necessary to determine\ncomplete pigment\ncomposition\nand\nother\noptically\nimportant\ncharacteristics\nof\nexceptional\nphytoplankton\nblooms\nfor\nsuch\nplanktonic\ngroups\nas\nCoccolithophorids,\nTrichodesmium,\ndiatoms,\ncyanobacteria,\nor microflagellates.\nTo achieve valid comparisons\nbetween the ship\nand satellite\ndata, sharp horizontal\ngradients\nand\nsub-pixel patchiness\nmust be avoided, and accurate\nimage navigation\nrequires land anchor points near\nthe study site. Suitable\nlandmarks\nare usually\navailable\nin near-shore\ncoastal waters. The other\nconditions\nare difficult to meet in Case-2 water\nmasses,\nwhere\nmesoscale\nand\nsub-mesoscale\nvariability\nis typically\nvery\nstrong.\nSub-pixel\nvariations of no more than + 35% of the mean pixel\nchlorophyll\nwill be tolerated,\nbut variability\nmust\nbe measured and taken into account statistically\nin\nthe analysis (see below).\nFrom the above generalities,\n\nit is clear\n\nthat\n\nsignificant\nproblems\nare encountered\nin near-shore\ncoastal\nwaters\ncharacterized\nby\nsmall-scale\npatchiness\nand dynamic\nvariability\ndue to tidal\ncurrents. A particular problem occurs in the shallow\nareas that are influenced\nby strong tidal currents -areas that are normally well mixed during part of\nthe tidal cycle. In the slack water tidal phase,\nhowever,\na vertical\ngradient\nof the suspended\nmatter concentration\nmay form, which may cause\nproblems\nin relating water-leaving\nradiance to the\nconcentration\nof suspended\nmatter. During calm\nperiods with strong insolation,\neven water that is\nnormally well mixed can become stratified. In these\ncases, the formation\nof very dense phytoplankton\nblooms, such as red tides, can be observed.\nSuch\n\nwill\n\noccur\n\nin coastal\n\nseas\n\nwhen\n\nnutrient\n\nOne approach to sampling in this environment\nhas been suggested by R. Doerffer (pets. comm.).\nIn order to get a good statistical\nbase, water\nsamples are first taken in a random order within the\narea under research.\nThe concentrations\nderived\nfrom the satellite\nimage data are then compared\nwith the ground truth data by statistical parameters,\nsuch as the mean, median, standard deviation,\nand\nthe shapes of histograms\n(frequency\ndistribution).\nFor this type of statistical comparison,\nonly sections\nof satellite images that match the area covered by\nthe ship should be analyzed. Water samples and\nsatellite data should also be temporally\nconcurrent\nwithin the same tidal phase in order to avoid biases\ndue to temporal\nvariability.\nIn these regimes,\nanalyses to validate algorithms cannot be based on\nsatellite ocean color data directly, but must instead\nbe based\non\nwater-leaving\nradiance\nspectra\nmeasured\nin situ (Chapter\n9) or from a ship\n(Chapter 10). This approach has the advantage that\nwater samples and radiance spectra are taken nearly\nsimultaneously.\nUsing either flow-through\npumping systems or\nsystems towed outside the ship\'s wake, fluorometry\ncan be used to assess chlorophyll\npatchiness\nif\nfrequent,\ni.e., every\n10-15 minutes,\nchlorophyll\nfluorescence-yield\ncalibration\nmeasurements\nare\nperformed.\nTowed\nabsorption,\nscattering,\nreflectance,\nand beam transmission\nmeters can also\nbe used to characterize\nspatial variability.\nWithin a\nfew hours of the overpass, the ship should occupy\nseveral stations at random locations within a 2 x 2\nkm 2 area central to the area selected for comparison\nwith satellite data. Sampling stations placed across\na tidal front during a satellite overpass may help to\nidentify two different water masses even when the\nfront has moved. Comparisons\nbetween in situ and\nsatellite\ndata in patchy\ncoastal\nareas may be\nenhanced\nby using horizontal\nradiance\nprofiles\nmeasured\nfrom an aircraft flying at low altitude\n(Section\n8.9). Subsets of such airborne\nprofiles\nallow direct comparisons\nwith shipboard\ndata. A\ncorresponding\nprofile may then be extracted from\nthe satellite image data for a direct comparison\nto\nthe aircraft trackline profiles. In Case-2 situations,\nsuch direct radiometric\ncomparisons\nare valuable\nfor validating\nand tuning local algorithms,\nbut are\nnot appropriate\nfor satellite\nocean color sensor\nsystem validation per se.\nTo\nvalidate\nocean\ncolor\natmospheric\ncorrections,\nwater-leaving\nradiances\nmeasured\nin\n\n69\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean Sensor\nO Color\nValidation\nsitu from the ship should be compared with those\nderived\nfrom the satellite\ndata. Sample matching\nproblems\naside,\nCase-2\nwaters\nare\noften\ncharacterized\nby strongly\nvarying\npatchiness\nin\noptical\nproperties,\npigment\nconcentrations,\nand\nremote sensing reflectance\nat spatial scales smaller\nthan a single pixel resolution\nof any of the current\ngeneration\nof ocean color sensors. Because of the\nnonlinear\nrelationship\nbetween\nabsorption\nby\npigments,\nthrough\nbb(L)/a(_,),\nand\nnormalized\n\n8.3 RADIOMETRIC\nMEASUREMENTS\n\nremote-sensing\nreflectance\nRRSN(L), the pigment\nconcentration\nderived\nfrom\nspatially\naveraged\nsatellite\nradiance\ndata\nwill\nsystematically\nunderestimate\nthe true spatial average concentration\nby as much as a factor of 2 when sub-pixel\nvariability is significant.\nIt is, therefore, essential to\ndescribe sub-pixel scale variability in Case-2 waters\nboth\nstatistically\nand\nin terms\nof organized\nstructure. Such a description\nmay be accomplished\nthrough\nrapid sampling\nat closely\nspaced\nship\nstations in combination\nwith airborne ocean color\nor LIDAR\nmeasurements\n-- for this purpose,\ntrackline\ndata\nfrom\nlow\naltitudes\nand\nhighresolution\nimagery from high altitudes\nare both\nacceptable (Section 8.9).\nAbsorption\ncoefficients\nare large enough in all\nCase-2 waters to require instrument\nself-shading\ncorrections\nto Lu(0, L), even though the correction\nmodel\n(Gordon\nand\nDing\n1992)\nhas\nbeen\nexperimentally\nverified\nonly for the case where\na(L)r is less than 0.1 (Section 9.4). In extreme Case2 waters, large values of spectral absorption\nmay\nconfine the first optical attenuation\ndepth to the top\n1--2 m, where it is difficult\nto measure remote\nsensing reflectance\nin situ. The influence of ship shadow is\nbest characterized\nin terms of attenuation\nlength\n1/I_(2_) (Gordon 1985). Because Lw(_.) is required\nwith an uncertainty\nof 5% or better, the protocol\nrequires that vertical profiles be measured outside\nthe effects of ship perturbation\nto the radiant energy\nfield. To accomplish\nthis, the instrument must be\ndeployed\nfrom the stern, with the sun\'s relative\nbearing aft of the beam. Yet a better approach is to\ndeploy\na free falling,\nprofiling\nradiometer\nwell\naway from the ship on an umbilical tether.\nEstimates of the minimum distance away from\nthe ship, under conditions of clear sunny skies, are\ngiven below.\nThe\ndistances\nare expressed\nin\nattenuation\nlengths to minimize error. The Mueller\nand Austin (1995) protocols related to above-water\nmeasurements\nof\nwater-leaving\nradiance\nand\nremote-sensing\nreflectance\nshould not be followed\nunder any circumstances.\nThe above-water\nmethods\nfor determining\nnormalized\nremote-sensing\nreflectance\n(NRSR), as\ndescribed\nabove, and their associated\nuncertainty\nbudgets, have been discussed\nat length in several\nmeetings and workshops over the last few years, as\nwell as in the literature cited here. The first\ntype is a photometric\nmeasurement\nof the direct\nsolar beam to determine\nthe optical thickness of the\natmosphere.\nThe intensity of the solar beam can be\nmeasured\ndirectly,\nor obtained\nindirectly\nfrom\nmeasurements\nof diffuse global upper hemispheric\nirradiance.\nThe second type\nis a measurement\nof\nthe solar aureole and sky radiance distribution\nusing\na CCD camera, or a scanning radiometer viewing in\n\nRADIANCE\nSYSTEMS\n\nSCANNING\n\nThe technology\nof ground-based\natmospheric\naerosol\nmeasurements\nusing sun photometry\nhas\nchanged substantially\nsince Volz (1959) introduced\nthe first hand-held\nanalog instrument\nalmost four\ndecades\nago. Modern\ndigital units of laboratory\nquality\nand field hardiness\ncollect\ndata more\naccurately\nand quickly and are often equipped\nfor\nonboard processing\n(Schmid et al., 1997; Ehsani,\n1998, Forgan, 1994; and Morys et al., 1998). The\nmethod\nused remains\nthe same, i.e., a detector\n\nand perpendicular\nto the solar principal plane.\nFrom the two types of measurements,\nthe\noptical properties\nof aerosols,\nhighly variable\nin\nmeasures through a spectral filter the extinction\nof\nspace and time, can be derived. Because of the high\ndirect beam solar radiation according\nto the Beervariability,\nthe aerosol properties\nshould be known\nLambert-Bouguer\nlaw:\nat the time of satellite overpass. Atmospheric\noptics\nmeasurements,\nhowever, are not easy to perform at\nsea, from a ship or any platform. Air mass is\ncalculated as\n\nM =_cos\n[\nwhere\n\nn\'0\xc2\xb0 +0.15"(93.885-0o)\n180 \xc2\xb0\nthe\n\nsun\n\nzenith\n\nangle\n\n\'253\n\nt-I\n\n, (11.3)\n\n0o is expressed\n\nin\n\ndegrees.\nSky-scanning\nspectral\nradiometers\nthat\nmeasure the spectral sky radiance at known angular\ndistances from the sun have expanded\nthe aerosol\nknowledge\nbase. They provide, through inversion\nof the sky radiance,\naerosol physical\nproperties,\nsuch as size distribution,\nand optical properties,\nsuch as the aerosol\nscattering\nphase function\n(Nakajima\net al., 1983, 1996; Tanr6 et al., 1988;\nShiobara\net al., 1991; Kaufman\net al., 1994;\nDubovik\net al., 2000; and Dubovik\nand King,\n2000). The inversion technique\nto calculate these\naerosol\nproperties\nrequires\nprecise\naureole\nmeasurements\nnear the solar disk and good straylight rejection.\nHistorically\nthese systems\nare\ncumbersome,\nnot weather hardy and expensive. The\nCIMEL\nand\nPREDE\n(French\nand\nJapanese\nmanufacturers\nrespectively)\nsun and sky scanning\nspectral\nradiometers\novercome\nmost\nof such\nlimitations,\nproviding\nretrievals\nof aerosol\nand\nwater\nvapor\nabundance\nfrom\ndirect\nsun\nmeasurements,\nand of aerosol\nproperties\nfrom\nspectral\nsky radiance\nmeasurements.\nSince\nthe\nmeasurements\nare\ndirectional\nand\nrepresent\nconditions of the total column atmosphere,\nthey are\ndirectly\napplicable\nto satellite\nand\nairborne\nobservations,\nas well as to studies of atmospheric\nprocesses.\nOwing to a sophisticated\ntracking system\nwith fast responding\nmotors, the PREDE can be\ninstalled onboard a ship, or other moving platform,\nto monitor aerosol optical properties\nat sea. In the\nfollowing,\nwe focus on the CIMEL system, since\nthe measurement\nprotocols\nare similar for both\nCIMEL and PREDE systems.\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nsun\nand\nsky\nspectral\nradiometer.\nAt\neach\nwavelength,\nthis instrument\nhas approximately\na\n1.2 \xc2\xb0 field-of-view\n(full angle) and filtered solar\naureole and sky radiance. The 33 cm collimators\nwere designed\nfor 10 .5 stray-light\nrejection\nfor\nmeasurements\nof the aureole 3 \xc2\xb0 from the sun. The\nrobot mounted sensor head is pointed at nadir when\nidle\nto prevent\ncontamination\nof the optical\nwindows\nfrom rain and foreign\nparticles.\nThe\nsun/aureole\ncollimator\nis protected\nby a quartz\nwindow,\nallowing\nobservation\nwith a ultraviolet\nenhanced silicon detector with sufficient signal-tonoise for spectral observations\nbetween\n300 and\n1020 nm. The sky collimator has the same 1.2 \xc2\xb0 field\nof view, but uses an order of magnitude\nlarger\naperture-lens\nsystem to improve dynamic range for\nmeasuring the sky radiance. The components\nof the\nsensor\nhead\nare\nsealed\nfrom\nmoisture\nand\ndesiccated\nto prevent\ndamage\nto the electrical\ncomponents\nand interference\nfilters.\nEight\nion\nassisted deposition interference filters are located in\na filter wheel rotated by a direct drive stepping\nmotor.\nA thermistor\nmeasures\nthe temperature\nof\nthe detector,\nallowing\ncompensation\nfor any\ntemperature dependence\nin the silicon detector.\nA polarization\nmodel of the CE-318\nis also\nused in SIMBIOS.\nThis version executes the same\nmeasurement\nprotocol\nas the standard model, but\nmakes additional hourly measurements\nof polarized\nsky radiance at 870 nm in the solar principal plane\n(Table 11.1 and 11.2).\nInstallation\nThe installation\nprocedures\nfor the CIMEL\ninstrument\nare summarized\nbelow. More detailed\ninformation\nis available from the AERONET\nweb\npage (http://aeronet.gsfc.nasa.gov:\n8080).\nThe site should have a clear horizon\n\nand be\n\nrepresentative\nof the regional aerosol regime. The\nbasic assembly is relatively simple to mount. The\ncables are labeled clearly and most fit only in one\nplace. Once the robot is assembled,\nit should be\noriented\nso the zenith motor casing is pointing\nroughly east (the metal claw to which the sensor\nhead is attached, then points to the west). The round\nconnector end of the data cable should be attached\nto the sensor head, and the flat connector\nshould be\nplugged into the white CIMEL control box. Strap\nthe sensor head to the robot metal claw using the\nsilver metal band. Make sure that the face of the\n\nDescription\n\nsensor head is flush with\nclaw. Also, ensure\nthat\ncollimator cross-section\nis\nof the zenith motor casing\nrobot itself is level. Do\n\nThe\nCIMEL\nElectronique\n318A\nspectral\nradiometer,\nmanufactured\nin Paris, France,\nis a\nsolar powered,\nweather hardy, robotically\npointed\n\n109\n\nthe edge of the metal\nthe long axis of the\nperpendicular\nto the axis\nand claw. Verify that the\nnot use the embedded\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olorensor\nO\nC S\nbubble level on top\nbubble level on top\nrobot tubular body\nThis should be level\n\nof the robot. Place the supplied\nof the flat ledge of the central\n(below the sensor head motor)\nin both the NIS and E/W axes.\n\nValidation\n\n2.9, and 2.11). Refer to the version most similar to\nyour particular\ntransmitter.\nOne may operate the\nVITEL display by using the control buttons. To\ninitiate an action, press the "SET-UP"\nbutton, then\npress the "SCROLL" button repeatedly\nto view the\ncategories in the current menu level. To choose any\nsubcategory,\npress the "SELECT\'\nbutton when the\ndesired feature is shown in the display window.\nTo\nchange\na parameter\nuse the right 2 buttons\n"CHANGE"\nand "ENTER\'".\nAt any time, one may\nreturn to the previous\n(higher)\nmenu level by\npressing the "SET-UP" button.\n\nVerify that the CIMEL control box "TIME" and\n"DATE"\nare correct, i.e., that they agree with the\nVITEL transmitter\nclock. If the Time or Date is\nwrong, the CIMEL\nwill not find the sun on a\n"GOSUN" command.\nNext, put the CIMEL\nin manual mode using\nthe white control box display screen. In Manual\nmode, the main screen reads: "PW MAN SCN\nVIEW". Do a "PARK" procedure.\nWhen "PARK"\nis complete\nthe sensor head collimator\nshould be\npointing down, perpendicular\nto the ground. Place\nthe bubble level on the top of the metal claw arm\nand verify that this is level. If not, loosen the zenith\nbolt\'s hex nut (below the permanent bubble level on\nthe top of the robot) and level it by rotating the\nzenith motor casing with your hand. Re-tighten\nthe\nzenith\nnut tightly.\nImportant:\nPerform\nanother\n"PARK" procedure,\nor two, and make sure it is in\nfact level.\n\nMeasurement\n\nProtocols\n\nThe\nradiometer\nmakes\nonly\ntwo\nbasic\nmeasurements,\neither direct\nsolar flux, or sky\nradiance.\nEach type of measurement\ninvolves\nseveral programmed\nsequences.\nDirect sun measurements\nare made in eight\nspectral bands distributed\nbetween 340 and 1020\nnm (440, 670, 870, 940 and 1020 nm are standard).\nEach\nmeasurement\nrequires\napproximately\n10\nseconds. A sequence of three such measurements\nare taken 30 seconds\napart creating\na tripiet\nobservation\nper wavelength.\nTriplet Observations\nare made during morning and afternoon\nLangley\ncalibration\nsequences\nand at standard\n15-minute\nintervals\nin between\n(Table\n15.1).\nThe time\nvariation of clouds is typically much greater than\nthat of aerosols, and therefore significant\nvariation\nin the triplets\nmay be used to screen\ncloud\ncontaminated\nmeasurements\nfrom\nthe\ndata.\n\nUsing the right 2 buttons, change the display to\nread "GOSUN".\nSelect "GO" to initiate. The sensor\nhead should point to the sun. The hole at the top of\nthe\ncollimator\nshould\nallow\nthe\nsunlight\nto\nilluminate\nthe marker\nspot at the base of the\ncollimator. When the bright spot is on the mark, the\ninstrument is aligned. If it is off to the left or right,\nrotate the robot base to align it. After you rotate the\nrobot, you will need to verify that the robot is still\nlevel as before. Park the instrument and perform\nanother "GOSUN"\nto check that the alignment\nis\nstill good. If not, ensure that the robot is level, and\nthat the sensor head is level when manually parked.\nOne note: when you level the sensor head and do a\n"GOSUN",\nrepeat this process a few times to be\nsure of the alignment.\nThe first "GOSUN"\nafter\nleveling is often not correct, because moving the\nsensor head while leveling can temporarily offset\nthe robot\'s zeroing point. Re-parking\nthe sensor and\ndoing a second "GOSUN"\nshould yield a more\naccurate alignment.\nRepeat this procedure\nuntil the\nalignment\nremains\naccurate\nand consistent\non\nrepetition.\nPress "PW" then increment to 4, and place the\ninstrument\nin "AUTO"\nmode. It is assumed that particles in the range 0.050.6 lam are fine mode and those in the range 0.6-15\nlam are coarse mode aerosols\n(Dubovik\net al.,\n2000). This definition is not completely\ncorrect in\nall size distributions.\nNevertheless,\nexperience\nhas\nshown it to hold true in the majority of practical\n\n(in lam-\n\n3/lam\'2), the volume particle size distribution\nin the\nrange of sizes 0.05 lam < r < 15 lain, and the\nvolume\nconcentration,\nvolume\nmean\nradius,\nstandard deviation, and effective radius for total (t),\nfine (f), and coarse (c) modes.\nNote that the fine and coarse mode variables\ncan be\n\nSmirnov\n\nL AOT & t\n\nvariability\nthat greater\n\nat 440, 670, 870, and 1020\n\n113\n\nof the r_ (2),\ntemporal\n\nwith the assumption\n\nvariance\n\nbeing\n\nin r a is due to the\n\nOcean Optics\n\nProtocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\npresence of clouds.\nThe first filter is a check of the\nvariability of the three Ta values measured within a\n\nOne of the most important\nareas of the sky\nradiance distribution\nto measure is the area near the\n\none-minute\n\nhorizon, opposite the sun, in the principal plane (the\nplane containing\nthe sun and the zenith direction).\nThis portion of the sky contains information\non the\nlarge scattering\nangle portion of the atmospheric\naerosol phase function, and is very important\nfor\ndetermining\nthe aerosol optical properties\nrelevant\nto atmospheric correction for ocean color satellites.\nThe second very important region of the sky is\nthe solar aureole, the region near the sun. Because\nthe aerosol scattering\nphase function\nis strongly\npeaked in the forward direction, information\nin this\nregion\nis important\nin determining\nthe aerosol\nsingle scattering albedo. Techniques\nfor converting\nsky radiance\nmeasurements\nto aerosol properties\nhave been described\nin Wang and Gordon (1993),\nGordon and Zhang (1995) and Zhang and Gordon\n(1997a, b).\nAn example\nof a camera\nsystem for sky\nradiance\ndistribution\nis described\nin Voss and\nZibordi\n(1989). The system described\nhas been\nupgraded, for greater dynamic range, with a cooled\nCCD array. The basic system consists of a fisheye\nlens, a spectral/polarization\nfilter changer,\nand a\ndigital camera. To block direct sunlight from hitting\nthe array, an occulter\nis manually\nadjusted\nto\nshadow the fish-eye lens. The size of the occulter is\n\nminimum\nminute\n\nperiod.\n\nIf\n\nand\n\nmaximum\n\ninterval\n\nis greater\n\nthe\n\ndifference\n\nL (A)\n\nwithin\n\nbetween\nthis\n\none\n\nthan 0.02 (for r_ less than\n\n0.667) or 0.03 _\'a (for _\'_ greater than 0.667) then\nthe\nmeasurement\nis\nidentified\nas\ncloud\ncontaminated.\nThen\nthe time series\nof the\nremaining\n\nr, (A)\n\nare analyzed\n\nfor the presence\n\nrapid changes\n\nor spikes\n\nin the data.\n\non the second\n\nderivative\n\nof the logarithm\n\nof\n\nA filter based\nof z\'_(2)\n\nas a function of time is employed\nto identify rapid\nvariations\nwhich are then filtered as observations\naffected by cloud.\nOther secondary\norder cloud\nscreening and data quality checks are also made and\nthese are described\nin detail in Smirnov\net al.\n(2000).\nUnscreened\ndata is fully available from the\nAERONET\nhomepage.\nAutomatic cloud screening\nof the almucantar\nand principal plane data is done\nby checking the distributions\nof data about the solar\ndisc for symmetry and smoothness.\n\n11.2 SKY RADIANCE\nDISTRIBUTION\nCAMERA\nSYSTEMS\nCamera systems for sky radiance distribution\nare useful to collect the entire hemisphere\nof sky\nradiance data in a quick manner. The resulting data\nimages\nusually\ncontain\nthe sun, so that the\nmeasurement\ngeometry\ncan\nbe\ndetermined\naccurately and unambiguously.\nAlso images can be\nchecked\nfor\ncloud\ncontamination\nand\nother\nmeasurement\nartifacts more easily than can be done\nwith data from scanning systems. The limitation of\ncamera systems is that the dynamic range of the\nwhole scene must be contained\nin each image.\nTherefore,\nthe camera\nsystem must have large\ndynamic range and there has to be a method of\nattenuating\nthe direct sunlight before it strikes the\nimaging optics. To get a complete\nsky radiance\ndistribution,\nincluding the solar aureole, it may be\nnecessary\nto have an auxiliary system to measure\nthe sky radiance\nnear the sun (Ritter and Voss,\n\n2000).\nIn addition, a sky radiance system, fitted with\npolarizers,\ncan measure\nthe Stokes\nparameters\ndealing\nwith linear polarization\n(Voss and Liu,\n1997). These additional\nparameters\nare useful for\ninvestigating\nthe polarization\nproperties\nof the\natmospheric\naerosols,\nand improving\nthe aerosol\noptical models.\n\napproximately\n5:20 \xc2\xb0 of the almucantar\nwhen the\nsun is at 60 \xc2\xb0 zenith angle; the effect of the occulter\nis obvious in data images shown in Liu and Voss\n(1997). Four spectral filters select the wavelength\nrange to be measured.\nPolarization\nfilters are used\nto collect 3 planes of polarization\nin data images.\nThese images can be combined\nto determine\nthe\nlinear polarization stokes vectors.\nMeasurement\n\nProtocols\n\nObviously the first order requirement\nis that the\nfield\nof view of the camera\nsystem\nbe as\nunobstructed\nas possible, and that the measurement\nsite be located in an appropriate\nplace-with\nrespect\nto the ships stack exhaust. If the whole field of view\ncannot be clear (as is usually the case), then one\nshould try for a clear hemisphere,\nwhere data\nbetween obstructions\nin the other hemisphere\ncan\nbe used for checking the sky symmetry.\nAs the desired\nobjective\nis the\naerosol\nscattering parameters,\nthe sky must also be cloud\nfree. Clouds cause two problems. The first is easy\nto detect and is the direct effect of having the bright\ncloud in the scene (in particular on the almucantar\nor principle\nplane).\nAlmost\nany\ncloud\nwill\noverwhelm the effect of aerosols in determining\nthe\nsky radiance. This effect of clouds is usually quite\n\n114\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor\nO\nC Sensor\nValidation\nevidentin the sky radiance\nimage. he second\nT\n\nVoss and Liu (1997). Basically the Mueller matrix\nof the camera system is described\nas interacting\nwith the Stokes vector of the skylight. There are\nthree orientations\nof a linear polarizer in the system\nproviding\nthree\nseparate\nMueiler\nmatrices\ndescribing\nthe camera\nsystem.\nFor each sky\ndirection\n(a pixel in the camera images),\nthese\nMueller\nmatrices\nand the resultant\nintensities\n\nproblem is the indirect effect of clouds, while not\ndirectly causing a problem, shadowing aerosols and\nreducing the skylight caused by aerosol scattering.\nThis second effect is more difficult to handle and\nplaces a more stringent requirement\non the state of\ncloudiness\nduring a measurement\nsequence.\nThis\neffect\ncan often\nbe quite\nvisible\nwhen\nthe\natmospheric\naerosol loading is high, causing light\nbeams to be evident in the aerosol layer. For these\nreasons, measurements\nwith clouds present should\nbe avoided if at all possible.\nThe maximum scattering angles existing in the\nsky radiance distribution\noccur near the horizon in\nthe principle\nplane opposite the sun. For a given\nsolar zenith angle, the maximum scattering angle is\ngiven by adding n/2 to the solar zenith angle. Since\nknowledge\nof the aerosol phase function at large\nscattering\nangles is important for the atmospheric\ncorrection\nprocess,\nmeasurements\nof the sky\nradiance distribution\nshould be taken when the sun\n\nmeasured by the camera form a set of simultaneous\nequations\nwith the unknowns being the sky Stokes\nvectors. For each pixel, these equations are inverted\nto obtain the Stokes vector of the skylight. While\nthese images have been evaluated qualitatively\n(Liu\nand Voss, 1997), work is currently being done to do\nmore quantitative\ninversions following the methods\nof Zhang and Gordon (1997b).\n\n11.3 HAND-HELD\nPHOTOMETERS\n\nis at large zenith angles. The optimum\nangle is a\ncompromise\nbetween getting large scattering angles\nand working\ntoo close to the horizon\nwhere\nmultiple\nscattering\neffects are large (because\nof\nlong optical paths through the atmosphere).\nA solar\nzenith angle of 600 has been chosen as optimum,\nbecause of these constraints.\nConcurrent\nwith\nthe\nsky\nradiance\nmeasurements,\nit is important\nto measure\nthe\naerosol optical depth.\nBy combining\nthe aerosol\noptical\ndepth and sky radiance\ndistribution,\nthe\naerosol scattering\nproperties\ncan be determined,\ntogether with the single scattering\nalbedo of the\naerosols\n(Wang and Gordon,\n1993; Gordon and\nZhang, 1995; Zhang and Gordon, 1997a).\nData Analysis\n\nThese instruments\ncost-effective\nmeans\n\nSUN\n\noffer the simplest and most\nto collect data on aerosol\n\noptical thickness\nat sea. They are based on the\nmeasurement\nof the solar beam intensity,\nand\ntherefore,\nthe direct\natmospheric\ntransmittance.\nFrom this transmittance,\nafter proper correction\nfor\nattenuation\nby air molecules,\nthe aerosol optical\nthickness\nmay be obtained\n(Equation\n15.1). The\ntechnique\nis straightforward\nin principle.\nIt is\ndifficult for an observer to point the photometer\nat\nthe sun accurately from a moving platform, but this\ndifficulty is obviated with modern-day\ninstruments.\nThe interest of these instruments\nalso resides in the\nfact that,\nthickness\n\nin most of the oceans, aerosol\nmeasurements\nat the time of\n\noptical\nsatellite\n\noverpass\nare sufficient\nto verify the atmospheric\ncorrection of ocean color (Schwindling\net al. 1998).\nThey allow one to estimate,\nvia the Angstrom\ncoefficient,\nthe "pseudo"\nphase function\nof the\naerosols (the product of the single-scattering\nalbedo\nand the phase\nfunction),\na key atmospheric\ncorrection variable.\n\nProtocols\n\nData reduction of the sky radiance data is very\nstraightforward,\nand is described\nin Voss and\nZibordi (1989).\nBasically with camera images, the\ndata reduction\nprocess\nconsists of simple image\nprocessing.\nEach image is multiplied by an absolute\ncalibration\nfactor and by an image that corrects for\ncamera\nlens roll-off.\nThis last factor is very\nimportant\nwith a fisheye lens, as the important\nportion of the The BNL marine version uses a slightly\nmodified\nversion\nof the\ndetector\nused\nfor\ncontinental\napplications.\nIt has seven channels: one\nbroadband\nsilicon detector\nand six ten-nm-wide\nchannels at 415, 500, 610, 660, 870, and 940 nm.\nModifications\nto the detector\ncircuitry\nused for\ncontinental\napplications\nare necessary because the\nresponse time of the original circuitry is too slow\nfor use on a moving ship. If the response\ntime of\nthe detector is too slow, wave action may cause the\norientation\nof the radiometer to change appreciably\nduring the time the shadow-band\nis occulting\nthe\nsun. The rotation of the shadow-band\nitself must be\nsufficiently\nfast for the same reason. The marine\nversion of the shadow-band\nradiometer\nis hereafter\n\nfront of the solar disk. Two sun photometer designs\nare commonly\nused to measure EN(A): a narrowbeam detector\nmechanically\npointed\nat the solar\ndisk and a wide-field-of-view\nradiometer\nwith a\n\nreferred to as the BNL Fast-Rotating,\nShadow-band\nRadiometer\n(FRSR).\nImplicit in this terminology\nis\nthat the FRSR\nis a multi-filter\nor "spectral"\nradiometer.\n\nsolar occulting\napparatus.\nThe first type of sun\nphotometer\nrequires careful angular positioning and\ncan provide\nadditional\ninformation\nabout\nthe\nforward\nscattering\nphase\nfunctions\nthat\nhelp\ncharacterize\nthe aerosol constituents.\nIn contrast, a\n\nThe response of the silicon cell in the detector\nused for continental\napplications\nis faster than one\nmillisecond,\nyet the internal\npreamplifiers\nhave\nintegrating\nlow-noise\namplifiers\nthat slow the\noverall response. Imagery from different ocean color\nsensors can now be processed by a single software\npackage using the same algorithms,\nadjusted\nby\ndifferent\nsensor spectral\ncharacteristics,\nand the\nsame ancillary\nmeteorological\nand environmental\ndata. the\nvolume\nabsorption\ncoefficients\nfor\nparticles,\nphytoplankton,\nphotosynthetic\npigments,\ndepigmented\nparticles\nand soluble\nmaterial\nare\ndescribed.\nMethods\nare based on prior of\nuncertainty\n\nthat\n\nwarrant\n\nT. pseudonana,\n\nfurther\n\nresearch.\n\nThe most widely used approach\nto estimate\nabsorption by particulate\nmatter involves analysis\nof the particles (1981) for diverse ocean\nenvironments,\nincluding oligotrophic\nand eutrophic\nregions.\nOther field reports can be found in the\nreferences\nlisted in more recent articles (Carder et\nal., 1989a, 1989b; Blough et al., 1993; Vodacek et\nal., 1996; Hoge et al., 1993; Nelson et al., 1998;\nD\'Sa et al., 1999).\nEstimating\nthe quantitative\nabsorption\nof soluble material is rather direct, but\nhas limits due to the very small signal for short\npathlengths\nroutinely employed (usually 10 cm). A\ndifficult issue is quality control of reference water\nand specification\nof an appropriate\nblank.\nA\nsummary of average blanks from several cruises is\nshown in Figure 2.\n\nestimates\nof ap were reported\nby Mitchell\nand\nKiefer (1988b) and Bricaud and Stramski (1990).\nMore detailed empirical results to correct for path\nlength amplification\nwere reported\nby Mitchell\n(1990) for various filter types and diverse cultures\nranging from 2 _m coccoid cyanobacteria\nto 10-20\n\n0.006\n\nI.tm diatoms,\nchrysophytes\nand dinoflagellates.\nCleveland\nand Weidemann\n(1993) and Tassan and\nFerrari (1995) found the empirical relationships\nof\nMitchell (1990) were consistent\nwith similar types\nof phytoplankton,\nbut Moore et al. (WH8103) and Prochlorococcus\nmarinus that were about half the size of the smallest\n\n0.005\n0.1304\n0.003\n_\n0\n\n0.002\n0.001\n\n0\n\ncells studied by Mitchell (1990; see also Table 2).\nSimilar results were obtained by Allali et al. (1995), however, found no statistical\n\nseven diffuse attenuation\ndepths, ln(E(0)/E(z))=7),\nto provide a basis for relating the spectroscopic\nmeasurements\nof absorption\nto in situ profile\nmeasurements.\nSamples\nshould\nbe\ndrawn\nimmediately\nfrom the in situ sampling bottles into\nclean sampling bottles using clean Tygon tubing. If\nNiskin bottles will not be sampled immediately,\nprecautions\nmust be taken to ensure large particles\nthat settle are resuspended.\nThis can be done by\ntransferring\nall water from the Niskin to a bottle or\ncarboy larger than the total volume of the Niskin so\nthat the entire water sample can be mixed (invert\nbottle numerous times to mix by turbulence),\nor by\ndraining a small amount of water from the Niskin\nand manually\ninverting\nthe entire Niskin prior to\nsub-sampling.\nSample bottles should be kept cool\n(ideally near in situ temperatures),\nand dark prior to\nsample\npreparations.\nPreparations\nshould\nbe\ncompleted\nas soon as possible after sampling,\nbut\nno later than several hours after the vertical profile\nwas acquired.\n\ndifference\nbetween GF/F and 0.2 tam filters for\nchlorophyll\nand\nproductivity\nmeasurements.\nVacuum pressures below -5 inches of mercury, i.e.,\n-120 mm Hg, are recommended\nto reduce the\nchances of particle breakage.\nThe absorption\nof the\nparticle fraction with 0.22-0.7 _tm diameter can be\nselectively\ndetermined\nby measuring\nthe GF/F\nfiltrate deposited on a 0.22 _rn Millipore cellulose\nacetate membrane filter (Ferrari and Tassan, 1996),\nFiltration volume should be adjusted\nto keep\nthe samples in the optical density range that is ideal\nfor the pathlength\namplification\ncorrections.\nGlass\nfiber, cellulose acetate, and other strongly diffusing\nfilters have large scattering\ncoefficients,\nwhich\nincreases the optical path length of photons in the\nmeasurement\nbeam.\nMitchell\n(1990) studied the\neffects\nof\nvariable\noptical\ndensity\non\nthe\nperformance\nof algorithms\nand recommended\nthat\noptical densities be in the range HPLC reconstruction\nmethods are\nuseful for improving\nour understanding\nof the\nrelative\ncontribution\nof phytoplankton\nto total\nabsorption,\nas well as the relative importance\nof\nphotosynthetic\nand photoprotective\npigments\nfor\nthe phytoplankton\nfraction.\nHowever,\nissues\nremain due to the lack of knowledge about the true\nin vivo\nextinction\ncoefficients\nof\nindividual\npigments,\nand the uncertainty\nin the degree of\npackaging\nof a polydisperse\nnatural\nparticle\nassemblage.\nEstimates of spectral mass extinction\ncoefficients\nfor these methods\nare provided in\nBidigare et al., (1990).\nThe method is useful if\npigment-packaging\neffects are negligible.\nFor this\n\nand Mitchell,\n\n1991; Moisan\n\nand\n\nThey\n\nfound\n\nat,s(_)<a_(A.),\n\nwhere\n\nthe\n\ndifference\nis\nattributable\nto photo-protective\npigments in the phytoplankton.\nIssues related to\nscaling\nthe relative\nfluorescence\nspectrum\nfor\ncultures to the chlorophyll\na absorption\nat the red\npeak have been discussed further by Johnsen et al.,\n(1994) and Moisan and Mitchell\n(1999).\nThe\n\nMethods have been proposed for the estimation\nof photosynthetically\nactive absorption.\nTotal\nphytoplankton\nabsorption\nis attributed primarily to\nthe sum of photosynthetic\nand photoprotective\npigments\n\n(Sosik\n\nMitchell,\n1999).\nAn improvement\nof Bidigare\'s\nmethod has been proposed by Babin et al. (1996)\nand applied\nby Allali et al. (1997) to natural\npopulations\nof the Equatorial Pacific. It consists of\npartitioning\nthe measured absorption spectrum into\nits\nphotosynthetic\nand\nnon-photosynthetic\ncomponents\nusing the HPLC information,\nand thus\ntakes into account the actual degree of packaging\nfor the population.\nFluorescence\nexcitation\nspectra for chlorophyll\na have been shown to be a good proxy of the\nspectral\nshape\nof the\nphotosynthetic\naction\nspectrum for many phytoplankton\ntypes (Neori et\nal., 1988).\nThus, they can represent\nthe relative\nabsorption\nspectrum\nof photosynthetically\nactive\npigments for some phytoplankton\ntypes. Sosik and\nMitchell (1995)have\nproposed a normalization\nof\nthe fluorescence\nexcitation spectrum to the red peak\nof absorption\nfor oceanic particulates\nto estimate\nabsorption\nby photosynthetically\nactive pigments,\n\nmethod\nassumes\nthat\npigments\nare\nequally\ndistributed\nbetween\nthe two photosystems\n(the\nfluorescence\nof PSI is undetectable\nat ambient\ntemperature).\nThis assumption\nis not valid for\nphycobiliprotein\nabsorption (Neori et al., 1988) and\nsome other phytoplankton\ngroups (Allali, 1997; M.\nBabin, unpublished\ndata).\n\n12.8 DATA\n\nREPORTING\n\nFor purposes of data reporting and archiving,\nthe absorption\ncoefficients\nwill be reported in rn-_\nand computed\nusing the equations\nsummarized\nabove. Several questions were addressed\nincluding\ncomparison\nof\ndifferent\nspectrophotometer\nsystems, evaluation of published\nmethods\nfor\ndetermination\nof\n13, sample\npreservation,\nnull\nabsorption\nwavelength\nsubtraction,\nde-pigmentation,\nand other\nissues.\nMany of these issues could benefit from further\nresearch on advanced protocols.\nSpectrophotometer\n\nexhibits instabilities,\nthe spectra\nregularly.\nSince\nall\nraw\ndeterminations\ndescribed\nhere\n\nconsiderations\n\nmust be repeated\noptical\ndensity\nare carried\nout\n\nrelative to a reference\n(e.g. blank filter, purified\nwater) proper treatment of the baseline and sample\nspectra\nshould provide\naccurate\noptical\ndensity\nresults.\nTo ensure accurate\nestimates\nof optical\ndensity, absorbing\nreference\nstandards\nshould be\nrun regularly\nrelative\nto air with the analytical\nequipment.\nAbsorption\nreference\nfilters\nfor\ndifferent\noptical\ndensities,\nand holmium\noxide\nfilters can be purchased\nfrom manufacturers\nor\nscientific\noptics supply companies\nto carry out\nthese performance\ntests.\nAt the Scripps\nworkshop,\nseveral researchquality\ninstruments\nprovided\nthe best\noverall\nperformance.\nThe Perkin Elmer Lambda\n18 was\nthe most consistent in the spectral range from 300800 nm for all sample\ntypes and measurement\ngeometries\nand is used in comparison\nto other\ninstruments\nlisted in Table 1.\nAt the Bigelow\nworkshop,\nthe moderately\npriced\nPerkin\nElmer\nLambda\n3 was used to compare\nvarious\nunits.\nScripps workshop results for one culture are shown\nin Figure 1 as an example of the type of results\nattained.\nIn general, with proper consideration\nof\ninstrumental\nbaselines\nand\ncare\nin\nsample\npreparations,\nall spectrophotometers\nthat could\ndetermine the optical density of suspensions\nagreed\nwell in the visible within routine analytical\nerror\n(Figure la). This was also true for determinations\nof cultures\nor natural particles\nconcentrated\non\nGF/F\nfilters\nboth\nusing\nstandard\noptics\nin\ncommercial\ngrating\nmonochromator\nsystems,\nor\n\nVarious\nspectrophotometer\noptions\nexist\nranging\nfrom commercially\navailable\nresearchquality\nsystems\nthat have scattered\ntransmission\nand reflectance\noptions to low-cost analytical units,\nto custom built instruments\nthat may be very simple\nor quite sophisticated.\nWith appropriate\ntraining\nand knowledge,\nhigh quality\nresults\nmay\nbe\nachieved\nwith diverse spectrophotometers.\nThe\ninvestigator\nmust weigh the merits of the flexibility\nof custom systems with the ease of operation\nof\ncertain robust analytical\nor research-quality\nunits.\nVarious instruments\nthat were evaluated during the\nNASA workshops\nare listed in Table 1. In terms of\nbaseline noise and stability, spectral precision and\nrange,\nand ability\nto measure\nboth filters and\nsuspensions,\nthe top performing\nspectrophotometers\nnot surprisingly\n- were the more expensive\ncommercial\nresearch-quality\nsystems.\nStill, many\nintermediate\ncost analytical\nunits performed\nwell\nthroughout\nthe spectral range. However, numerous\ninstruments\nexhibited problems with baseline noise,\nspectral\nrange,\nspectral\naccuracy\nand stability.\nOften several of these problems were found for an\ninstrument.\nTo carry out appropriate\nwork on\nparticle suspensions\nrequires either a high quality\nscattered transmission\naccessory or an integrating\nsphere.\nFor the workshops,\nthe analyses\non\nsuspensions\nwere\nlimited\nto the moderate\nto\nexpensive\ncommercial\nsystems that had integrating\nsphere accessories.\nVerification\nof the spectral\nand quantitative\naccuracy of the optical density estimates\nmust be\n\n139\n\nOcean Optics\n\ncollecting\naccessory\n\nProtocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\nthe energy with a scattered transmission\nor integrating\nsphere (Figure 1B). Separation of the total particulate\nabsorption\ncoefficient\nas measured\non glass-fiber\nfilters\n_ap)\ninto phytoplankton\n_a_) and nonphytoplankton\nor "detritar\'\ntad ) components\nis an\nimportant pre-condition\nfor using these absorption\ndata to validate\nocean\ncolor satellite\nproducts,\nincluding\npigment\nbiomass\nindexes and primary\nproductivity.\nEarly efforts to separate absorbing\ncomponents\nin natural samples included treatment\nwith organic solvents, UV radiation, and potassium\npermanganate\n(references\ncan be found in Shifrin,\n1988, and Bricaud and Stramski, 1990).\nMethods\nto partition\na e into its components\ncan be grouped\nby methodology.\nChemical\ntechniques\nextract\nor bleach\nthe more\nlabile\npigments on the filter, leaving refractory absorbing\nmaterial behind.\nThe treated filter is scanned again\nto retrieve\n\na a , which\n\nis then subtracted\n\nfrom ap to\n\nyield a_ (e.g., Kishino et al., 1985, Tassan and\nFerrari 1995a). Statistical techniques to decompose\ntotal particulate\nabsorption\nspectra into these two\ncomponents\nhave been proposed (e.g., Morrow et\nal., 1989, Bricaud\nand Stramski\n1990, Cleveland\nand Perry 1994).\nMathematical\nmethods are not\ntruly independent\nsince they are typically validated\nusing the results of chemical\nseparation\nmethods.\nMicrospectrophotometric\nobservations\nof individual\nparticles to estimate each component\ndirectly (e.g.,\nIturriaga et al., 1988; Iturriaga and Siegel 1989), are\ntime consuming\nand therefore\nnot amenable\nto\nroutine\nestimates,\nbut\nof\ngreat\nvalue\nin\nunderstanding\nthe details\nof particle\nabsorption\nwithin a sample.\nReconstruction\nof spectra from\nthe\nconcentration\nof\nHPLC-determined\nphytoplankton\npigments (e.g., Bidigare et al., 1990)\ncan be used but this method does not directly result\nin\n\nan\n\nestimate\n\nof\n\nOcean Color evaluated\nto\nassess differences\nand to provide recommendations\nfor common procedures.\nChemical methods are the most widely\n\nresulting\nfrom partitioning\nof the total particulate\nabsorption\ncoefficient\nusing\nchemical\nor\nmathematical\ntechniques are purely operational,\nas\nany extraction\nor bleaching\ntechnique\ndoes not\npurely\nselect\nfor (or\nagainst)\nphytoplankton\npigments.\nAny\nnon-phytoplankton\npigments\nextracted or bleached in a chemical method would\nthus result in an overestimation\nof a_, while any\nphytoplankton\npigments\nleft on the filter after\ntreatment would result in an overestimation\nof aa.\nMathematical\nmethods\nalso\ninvolve\nvarious\nassumptions\nleading to un-quantified\nuncertainties.\nThe a d spectrum\ngenerally\nhas a monotonically\nincreasing\nabsorption\nwith decreasing\nwavelength\nusually with a slight exponential\nform that is flatter\nthan soluble absorption.\nSince the goal is generally\nto get an estimate of phytoplankton\nabsorption,\nif\nthere is a residual chl a absorption\npeak in the red\nnear 675 nm the extraction\nprocess\nshould\nbe\nrepeated until the peak disappears.\nBleaching\nof\nthe organic pigments can also be accomplished\nfor\nsituations\nwith\ndifficult\nto extract\npigments\nincluding\nphycobilins\nor other chemically\npolar\npigments\nthat do not extract\nwell in methanol.\nVariations\nof this method include use of hot or\nboiling methanol and varying extraction times. Use\nof hot methanol has risks due to flammability,\nand\nvolatility.\nIf this process is used, extra precautions\nmust be taken.\nExtractive\nmethods\nsuch\nas methanol\nare\nfundamentally\ndifferent\nin action\nfrom sodium\nhypochlorite\n(NaCIO) used to bleach, rather than\nextract,\nphytoplankton\npigments.\nBleaching\ninvolves placing a small amount of 0.1% active\nchlorine solution onto the filter, then rinsing with\nwater. The NaCIO oxidizes the pigment molecules,\nmaking their light absorption\nnegligible.\nWater\nrinses\nthen remove\nthe excess\nNaCIO,\nwhose\nabsorption is negligible above 400 nm but increases\nsteeply below that wavelength.\nThis method was\nfound to be effective in situations where methanol\ncannot be used, as on cellulose membranes\nsuch as\nthe 0.22 micron Millipore filter, or on phycobilins.\nAlso this procedure\ncan be adapted\nfor use with\nparticulate\nsuspensions.\nSeveral\nchemical\nmethods\nfor\nextracting\npigments from marine particles collected on glassfiber filters were compared.\nTest samples included\npure\ncultures\nof Thalassiosira\nweissflogii\n(a\ndiatom),\nDunalieIla\ntertiolecta\n(a chlorophyte),\nSynechococcus\nstrain WH7805 (a Consideration\nof both\ntransmittance\nand reflectance\nin development\nof\ncorrections\nfor glass fiber filters\ncan improve\nmethods,\nespecially\nwhen\nparticle\nscattering\nintroduces\nlarge errors associated\nwith assignment\nof a near-IR null point.\nA\nmodification\ntransmission\nmethod\n\nof\n\nthe\nthat\n\ncurrent\ncorrects\n\nlightfor\n\nbackscattering\nwas described by Tassan and Ferrari\n(1995).\nThis technique\ncombines\nlight-transmission (T) and light-reflection\n(R) measurements,\ncarried out using an integrating\nsphere attached to\nthe\ndual-beam\nspectrophotometer.\nThe\ndata\nanalysis is performed\nby a theoretical\nmodel that\neliminates\nthe effect of light backscattering\nby the\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nnm is analyzed\nfor the determination\nof the\ncorrelation\n(Tassan\nand Ferrari,\n1998).\nAt the\nScripps\nworkshop,\nthe global error of the T-R\nmethod was comparable\nto the error yielded by the\nsimpler T method. Subsequent\nmodifications\nof the\nT-R experimental\nroutine\n(Tassan\nand Ferrari,\n1998;\nFerrari\nand Tassan,\n1999)\nyielded\na\nsignificant reduction of the experimental\nerror. The\nT-R method is particularly\nsuited for applications\nto\nsamples containing\nhighly scattering particles that\nare commonly\nfound in Case 2 waters.\nFigure 9\nillustrates\nthe results for a 13 relationship\nusing\nScripps Pier water with a considerable\namount of\nlight-scattering\ndetritus.\nThe T-R method, being\nmore complicated\nthan the T method, is affected by\na larger number\nof error sources\nthat must be\nconsidered.\nAlso, since the method requires a high\nquality spectrophotometer\nwith integrating\nsphere\nattachment\nthat is more costly and difficult\nto\nmaintain during many field campaigns,\nthis method\ndoes not yet have the widespread\nuse of the simple\nT method.\nStill, to the extent\nfeasible,\nmore\ninvestigators\nshould evaluate the advantages\nof the\nT-R methods.\n\n12.9 CONCLUSIONS\nSpectral absorption\nand backscattering\ngovern\nthe reflectance\nof the ocean.\nIn principle,\nit is\ntrivial\nto determine\nabsorption\ncoefficients\nat\nhyperspectral\nresolution\non water\nsamples\nwith\naccuracies\nof 20-30% after appropriate\npreparation\nand\nattention\nto optical\nanalysis\nand\ndata\nprocessing.\nSuch data is strongly recommended\nin\nsupport of ocean color science to allow a better\nunderstanding\nof ocean reflectance,\nand for various\nphotochemical\nand photobiological\napplications.\nStandard protocols for determining\nthe absorption\nby different\nfractions\nin seawater\nare described.\nLaboratory\ncomparisons\nof different\ninstruments\nand procedures at two NASA-sponsored\nworkshops\nhave led to several important conclusions\nregarding\nthe methods and a set of simple analytical protocols\nthat\nshould\nensure\nconsistent\ndata\nquality\nif\nproperly implemented.\n\nparticles.\nThe conversion of the optical density of\nthe sample filter measured by the T-R method, into\nthe equivalent\noptical\ndensity\nof the particle\nsuspension\nis obtained\nby means of an empirical\ncorrelation\nthat\nis determined\ncomparing\nthe\nscattering-free\npigment optical densities of particles\nin suspension and retained on the filter. Trees\n\n2\n\nof Hawaii,\nDiego\n\nLaboratory\n\nState\n\nHawaii\nUniversity,\n\nCalifornia\n\nwith a powerful\ntool for studying\nthe processes\naffecting\nthe\nphytoplankton\npigment\npool.\nPigment\ndistribution\nis useful\nfor quantitative\nassessment\nof\nphytoplankton\ncommunity\ncomposition\nand zooplankton\ngrazing activity.\nFor\nlow\nuncertainty\ndeterminations\nof\nchlorophylls\na, b, and c, chlorophyll\ndegradation\nproducts,\nand\ncarotenoid\npigments,\nHPLC\ntechniques\nare recommended.\nIt should be noted,\nhowever, that the reverse-phase\nCts HPLC method\nrecommended\nby the Scientific\nCommittee\non\nOceanographic\nResearch\n(SCOR)\n(Wright\net al.\n1991) is not capable\nof separating\nmonovinyl\nchlorophyll\na from divinyl\nchlorophyll\na, nor\nmonovinyl\nchlorophyll\nb from divinyl chlorophyll\nb. This method, therefore, only provides estimates\nof total chlorophyll\na and total chlorophyll\nb\nconcentrations,\nrespectively.\nProtocols for optically\nresolving\nmonovinyl\nchlorophyll\na and divinyI\nchlorophyll a are given below.\nDivinyl\nchlorophyll\na,\nthe\nmajor\nphotosynthetic\npigment found in Prochlorococcus,\naccounts for i0-60% of the total chlorophyll\na in\nsubtropical\nand tropical oceanic waters (Goericke\nand Repeta 1993, Letelier et al. 1993, Andersen et\nall 1996, Bidigare and Ondrusek 1996, and Gibbet\nal. 2000).\nDivinyl\nchlorophyll\na is spectrally\ndifferent\nfrom normal (monovinyl)\nchlorophyll\na\nand\nits\npresence\nresults\nin\na\nsignificant\noverestimation\nof total chlorophyll\na concentration\nas determined\nby the conventional\nHPLC methods\n(Goericke\nand Repeta 1993, Letelier et al. 1993,\nand Latasa et al. 1996). To avoid these errors, it is\nrecommended\nthat\nmonovinyl\nand\ndivinyl\nchlorophyll\na\nbe\nspectrally\nresolved,\nor\nchromatographically\nseparated,\nin order to obtain\nan unbiased\ndetermination\nof total chlorophyll\na\n(that\nis, total\nchlorophyll\na equals\ndivinyl\nchlorophyll\na plus monovinyl chlorophyll\na) for the\npurpose\nof ground-truthing\nsatellite\nocean color\nalgorithms\nand\nimagery.\nThese\nco-eluting\n\nMarine phytoplankton\nutilize chlorophyll\na as\ntheir\nmajor\nlight\nharvesting\npigment\nfor\nphotosynthesis.\nOther\naccessory\npigment\ncompounds,\nsuch\nas chlorophylls\nb and\nc,\ncarotenoids\nand phycobiliproteins,\nalso play a\nsignificant\nrole in photosynthesis\nby extending the\norganism\'s\noptical\ncollection\nwindow,\nthereby\nimproving\nabsorption\nefficiencies\nand adaptation\ncapabilities.\nThe important chlorophyll degradation\nproducts found in the aquatic environment\nare the\nchlorophyllides,\nphaeophorbides,\nand phaeophytins.\nThe\npresence,\nor\nabsence,\nof\nthe\nvarious\nphotosynthetic\npigments\nis used to separate the\nmajor\nalgal\ngroups,\nand\nto\nmap\nthe\nchemotaxonomic\ncomposition\nof phytoplankton\nin\nthe oceans.\nThe unique optical properties\nof chlorophyll\na\nhave been used to develop\nspectrophotometric\n(Jeffrey\nand Humphrey\n1975) and fluorometric\n(Holm-Hansen\net\nal.\n1965)\nmeasurement\ntechniques.\nWith the commercial\navailability\nof\nfluorometers\nfor\nroutine\nmeasurements\nof\nchlorophyll\na, this pigment became\na universal\nparameter\nin\nbiological\noceanography\nfor\nestimating phytoplankton\nbiomass and productivity.\nThese optical methods can significantly\nunder- or\noverestimate\nchlorophyll\na concentrations,\nbecause\nof the overlap of the absorption\nand fluorescence\nbands\nof co-occurring\nchlorophylls\nb and c,\nchlorophyll\ndegradation\nproducts,\nand accessory\npigments\n(Trees et al. 1985; Smith et al. 1987;\nHoepffner\nand Sathyendranath\n1992; Bianchi et al.\nI995; Tester et al. 1995).\nThe application\nof HPLC to phytoplankton\npigment analysis\nhas lowered the uncertainty\nfor\nmeasuring chlorophyll\na and pheopigments,\nas well\nas the accessory\npigments,\nsince compounds\nare\nphysically\nseparated\nand individually\nquantified.\nHPLC has provided oceanographers\n\n154\n\nZ\n\nOcean Optics\n\nProtocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\nresolve variability within at least the top optical\ndepth. The K (_, ,z), profiles over this layer will be\nused to compute optically\nweighted,\nnear-surface\npigment\nconcentration\nfor bio-optical\nalgorithm\ndevelopment\n(Gordon and Clark 1980).\nWhen possible, samples should be acquired at\nseveral depths distributed throughout the upper 200\nm of the water column [or in turbid water, up to\nseven diffuse\nattenuation\ndepths,\nIn((E(_,,0)/E(L\n,z))=7], to provide a basis for relating fluorescence\nsignals to pigment mass concentration.\nSamples should be filtered as soon as possible\nafter collection.\nIf processing must be delayed (>1\nhr), hold samples on ice or at 4\xc2\xb0C and protected\nfrom exposure to light. Use opaque sample bottles,\nbecause\neven\nbrief exposure\nto light\nduring\nsampling and/or storage might alter pigment values.\n\nchlorophyll\nspecies\ncan be resolved\nspectrally\nfollowing Cls HPLC chromatography\n(Wright et al.\n1991) and quantified using dichromatic equations at\n436 and 450 nm (Latasa et al. 1996). Alternatively,\nthese two chlorophyll\nspecies can be separated\nchromatographically\nand individually\nquantified\nusing\nthe Ca HPLC\ntechnique\ndescribed\nby\nGoericke and Repeta (1993). (Cis and Cs designate\ncolumn-packing\nmaterials used in HPLC.)\nThese protocols\nspecified\nbelow for HPLC\npigment analyses follow closely those prescribed in\nthe\nJGOFS\nCore\nMeasurement\nProtocols\n(UNESCO\n1.\n2.\n3.\n\n1994). Both sets of protocols\n\ninclude:\n\nUse of Whatman\nGF/F glass fiber\napproximately\n0.7 _m pore size;\nExtraction in aqueous acetone; and\nCalibration\nwith authenticated\nstandards.\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nfilters,\n\nFiltration\nThe present protocols\ndiffer from the JGOFS\nprotocols in one critical respect. Absorption\nof light\nin seawater,\nor any other medium, is a volumetric\nprocess,\neven\nthough\nthe volume\nabsorption\ncoefficient\nmay vary with the density\nof the\nmedium.\nFor ocean color and optical analyses,\ntherefore,\nthe concentrations\nin seawater\nof all\nphytoplankton\npigments shall be expressed in units\nof mass per unit volume of seawater, usually either\nin l_g L -_, or mg m 3. This differs from the JGOFS\nprotocols,\nwhich specify\nthat concentrations\nin\nseawater of all phytoplankton\npigments should be\nexpressed in ng kg l.\nIn addition to HPLC analyses, it is recommended\nthat the standard\nfluorometric\nmethodology\nused\nfor measuring\nchlorophylls\nand\npheopigments\n(Yentsch\nand Menzel\n1963, Holm-Hansen\net al.\n1965, and Strickland\nand Parson\n1972) also be\napplied to the same extracted pigment samples used\nfor HPLC\nanalysis.\nProtocols\nfor fluorometric\nmeasurements\nof chlorophyll\na and pheopigments\nare given here in Chapter 21. For a more in depth\nreview of guidelines\nfor measuring phytoplankton\npigments in oceanography\nsee Jeffrey et al. (1997)\n\nWhatman\nGF/F\nglass\nfiber\nfilters,\napproximately\n0.7 _tm pore size, are preferred\nfor\nremoving\nphytoplankton\nfrom water.\nThe glass\nfibers assist in breaking\nthe cells during grinding,\naccommodate\nlarger sample volumes, and do not\nform precipitate\nforms after acidification.\nInert\nmembrane\nfilters, such as polyester filters, may be\nused when size fraction\nfiltration\nis required,\nalthough it is recommended\nto also filter a replicate\nsample\nthrough\na GF/F to determine\nthe total\nconcentration\n(summing\nthe\nvarious\nsize\nfractionated\nconcentrations\nwill not produce\nan\naccurate\nestimate\nof the total, because\nof the\npotential\nfor cell disruption\nduring\nfiltration).\nTwenty-five\nmm diameter GF/F glass fiber filters\nshould\nbe used with vacuum\n(7-8 inches\nof\nmercury) or positive pressure\n(I-2 psi).\nPositive\npressure\nfiltration\nis recommended,\nbecause\nit\nfilters larger volumes of water at reduced filtration\ntimes. The only problem with vacuum filtration is\nthat unobservable\nair leaks may occur around the\nfiltration\nholder,\nand as a result\nthe pressure\ngradient across the filter is much less than what is\nindicated\non the vacuum gauge.\nWhen positive\nfiltration\nis used, any leakage\naround the filter\nholder results in observable dripping water.\nThere has been an ongoing discussion on filter\ntypes and retention efficiencies\nfor natural samples.\nPhinney\nand\nYentsch\n(1985)\nshowed\nthe\ninadequacy\nof GF/F filters for retaining chlorophyll\na in oligotrophic\nwaters,\nas did Dickson\nand\nWheeler (1993) for samples from the North Pacific.\nIn response\nto Dickson\nand Wheeler\n(1993),\nChavez et al. (1995) compared samples collected in\nthe Pacific\nOcean\nusing\nGF/F\nand 0.2 lam\nmembrane filters with small filtered volumes (100-\n\n13.2 SAMPLING\nPROTOCOLS\nFOR PHYTOPLANKTON\nPIGMENTS\nWater Samples\nWater samples should be taken using Niskin\nbottles at the site of, and simultaneously\nwith, the\nsurface in-water upwelled radiance and reflectance\nmeasurements,\nand at depth increments sufficient to\n\n155\n\nOcean Optics Protocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\n540 mL).\nTheir results\nshowed\na very close\nagreement\nbetween the two filter types with GF/F\nfilters having only a slightly positive 5% bias.\nFiltration\nvolume\ncan directly\naffect\nthe\nretention efficiency\nfor GF/F filters.\nParticles can\nbe retained by filters through a variety of ways such\nas filter sieving, filter adsorption,\nelectrostatic\nand\nvan der Waals attractions,\netc. (see the review by\nBrock\n1983).\nIt is known that Whotman\nGF/F\nfilters can retain particles much smaller than their\nrated pore size. With Nuclepore filters, when water\nflows through\nthe pores, streamlines\nare formed\nthat can align small particles\nlongitudinally\n(cell\ndiameter\nbecomes\nimportant\nwith these filters).\nGenerally,\nat small volumes (100-300\nmL) filter\nadsorption,\nand electrostatic\nand van der Waals\nattractions\nare important, whereas at larger volumes\n(> 2,000 mL) sieving dominates.\nThis has been\ntested in oligotrophic\nwaters off Hawaii in which\nsmall (< 500 mL) and large volumes (> 2-4 liters)\nretained similar amounts of chlorophyll\na, whereas\nintermediate\nvolumes\nhad lower concentrations.\nDuring several cruises\ndifferences\nin retention\n\nsp. Large zooplankton\ncan be removed\nfiltration using forceps.\nSample Handling and Storage\n\nm esotrophic,\n\noff the Hawaiian\nIslands,\nefficiencies\nwere found for\n\nand\n\n0.5-1\n\nliter\n\nfollowing\n\nSamples\nshould\nbe filtered\nas quickly\nas\npossible after collection and stored immediately\nin\nliquid nitrogen.\nLiquid nitrogen is the best method\nfor storing samples with minimum degradation\nfor\nshort, as well as, longer storage times (e.g. 1 year).\nPlacing samples in liquid nitrogen\nalso assists in\npigment extraction\nby weakening\nthe cell wall and\nmembrane\nduring this rapid temperature\nchange.\nUltra-cold freezers (-90\xc2\xb0C) can be used for storage,\nalthough they have not been tested for longer than\n60 days (Jeffrey et al. 1997).\nConventional\ndeep\nfreezers should\nnot be\nused for storing samples\nmore than 20 hours before transferring\nthem to an\nultra-cold\nfreezer,\nor liquid\nnitrogen.\nAgain,\nstorage of samples in liquid nitrogen immediately\nafter filtration is the preferred method.\nSamples\nshould be folded in half with the\nfiltered halves facing in. This eliminates\nproblems\nof rubbing particles off the filter during placement\nin sample containers\nand storage.\nThe easiest and\nleast expensive\nsample container is aluminum foil.\nCut small pieces of heavy duty aluminum foil into\napproximately\n4 cm squares.\nFold this in half and\nusing a fine-point permanent\nm_ker\nwrite a short\nsample identifier (e.g. first letter of the cruise and a\nsequential numbering scheme) on the foil. Writing\non the folded foil, prior to placement\nof the filter,\nalleviates problems of puncturing\nthe foil with the\nmarking pen, as well as improving the legibility of\nthe sample identifier.\nTake the folded filter and\nplace it in the aluminum foil. Fold the three open\n\nGF/F filters to be a function of sample volume;\nlarge sample volumes (2 and 4 liters) retained about\n18% more chlorophyll\na than replicate\n1 liter\nsamples.\nFiltration\nvolumes are usually limited by the\nconcentration\nof particles present in each sample.\nFor HPLC analysis it is important to filter as large\nof volume as possible so as to accurately\nmeasure\nmost of the major pigments.\nA qualitative check to\ndetermine whether a large enough volume has been\nfiltered\nis to count\nthe number\nof accessory\npigments\n(chlorophylls\nb,\ncl,\nc2, c_, and\ncarotenoids)\nquantified,\nexcluding\nchlorophyll\ndegradation\nproducts\n(Trees et al. 2000).\nMost\nalgal groups (excluding phycobiliprotein-containing\ngroups)\ncontain\nat least four\nHPLC-measurable\naccessory\npigments\n(see Jeffrey\net al. 1997).\nTherefore,\npigment samples that do not meet this\nminimum accessory pigment requirement\nmay have\ndetection\nlimit problems\nrelated to low signal-tonoise\nratios\nfor\nthe HPLC\ndetectors\nand/or\ninsufficient\nconcentration\ntechniques\n(e.g.\nlow\nfiltration\nvolumes).\nIt is recommended\nthat\ngenerally\nthe following\nvolumes\nbe filtered for\nthese water types: 3-4 liters for oligotrophic,\n1-2\nliters for\nwaters.\n\nOcean Color Sensor Validation\n\nsides to form an envelope that is _only slightly larger\nthan the folded filter (-3 cm x 1.5 cm).\nThis\nprotocol will minimize the size requirement\nof the\nstorage container.\nCryogenic\ntubes or HistoPrep\ntissue capsules can be used, although they require a\nlarger\nstorage\nspace\nand\nunless\nreused,\nare\nexpensive\nas\ncompared\nto\naluminum\nfoil.\nInformation\nregarding\nsample identification\nshould\nbe logged in a laboratory notebook.\n\n13.3 Three mL\nHPLC-grade\nacetone\nis added\nto each\ntube,\nfollowed\nby the addition of a fixed volume\nof\ninternal standard (typically 50 gL canthaxanthin\nin\nacetone).\nAlternatively,\ncanthaxanthin\nspiked\nHPLC-grade\nacetone solvent may be prepared\nin\nadvance,\nin a batch large enough for all samples,\nand 3 mL is added to each tube in a single step.\nSince GF/F filters retain a significant\namount of\nseawater following filtration (ca. 0.2 mL per 25 mm\nfilter),\nthe final\nacetone\nconcentration\nin the\npigment extracts is ~ 94% (acetone:water,\nvol:vol);\n\nfor each\n\nthe canthaxanthin\n\nsample,\n\nthe ratio\n\npeak area\n\nA Caatha ] A Cam_a\n\n"sro\n\nI-s_,_\n\nPrograms\n\nThere are currently\ntwo recognized\nHPLC\nmethods\nfor separating\nchlorophylls,\nchlorophyll\nderivatives\nand\ntaxonomically\nimportant\ncarotenoids.\nThe first method, which is recommended\nby\nSCOR\nand proposed\nby Wright\net al. (1991),\nseparates more than 50 chlorophylls,\ncarotenoids,\nand their derivatives\nusing\na ternary\ngradient\nsystem.\nThis HPLC method is described\nin detail\nin section 20.2.4. Briefly, pigments are separated on\na Spherisorb\nODS-2 Cj8 column\nusing a three\nsolvent\ngradient\nsystem\n[Solvent\nA:\n80:20\nmethanol:\n0.5 M ammonium\nacetate (v/v); Solvent\nB: 90:10 acetonitrile:\nwater (v/v); Solvent C: ethyl\nacetate]\nat a flow rate of 1 mL min a.\nThe\nseparation\nof the various pigments requires about\n30 minutes. Prior to injection, 1 mL of the aqueous\nacetone pigment extract\nis diluted with 0.3 mL\nHPLC-grade\nwater\nto increase\nthe affinity\nof\npigments for the column during the loading step.\nThis results\nin sharper\npeaks, allowing\ngreater\nloading\nthan can be obtained\nwith undiluted\nsamples.\nThis method does not separate monovinyl\nand divinyi chlorophylls\na and b. The presence of\ndivinyl chlorophylls\na and b, can cause errors if\nthey are not separated\neither physically\non the\ncolumn, or by a channels\nratio method\nfrom the\nmonovinyl forms. Latasa et al. (1996) showed that\nthe use of a single response\nfactor (only\nfor\nmonovinyl chlorophyll\na) could result in a 15-25%\noverestimation\nof total chlorophyll\na concentration\nif divinyl chlorophyll\na was present in significant\nconcentrations.\nAlthough\nmonovinyl\nand divinyl\nchlorophyll\na co-elute,\neach compound\nabsorbs\ndifferently at 436 nm and 450 nm and it is therefore\npossible to deconvolve\nthe absorption signals due to\nthese pigments (Latasa et al. 1996).\n\nExtraction\n\nby measuring\n\nand Gradient\n\nA s,_vl,\ncant_\n\nmay be\n\nused to adjust for sample to sample variations in the\nacetone:water\nratio. Samples\nare disrupted\nby\nsonication\nin darkness at 0\xc2\xb0C and allowed to extract\nat -20\xc2\xb0C for 24 h. Alternatively,\nthe cells can be\nmechanically\ndisrupted using a glass/Teflon\ntissue\ngrinder.\nThe ease at which the pigments are\nremoved from the cells varies considerably\nwith\ndifferent phytoplankton.\nIn all cases, freezing the\nsample filters in liquid nitrogen improves extraction\nefficiency.\nPrior to analysis, pigment extracts are\nvortexed\nand centrifuged\nto minimize\ncellular\ndebris.\nTo remove fine glass fiber and cellular\ndebris from the extract, as well as enhance the life\nexpectancy\nof the HPLC column, filter the extract\nthrough\n13ram PTFE\n(polytetrafluoroethylene)\nmembrane syringe filters (0.2 gm pore size).\n\n157\n\nOcean Optics\n\nProtocols\n\nFor Satellite\n\nOcean Color Sensor\n\ncan purchased\nfrom\nthe International\nAgency for 14C Determination,\nVKI Water\nQuality Institute, Agern All6 11, DK-2970\nHOrsholm,\nDenmark.\nThe concentration\nof all standards\nshould\nbe determined\n\nThe second method, which physically separates\nthe monvinyl\nand divinyl chlorophylls\na and b,\nchlorophyll\nderivatives\nand carotenoids,\nis that of\nGoericke\nand Repeta\n(1993).\nPigments\nare\nseparated\non a C8 column using a linear binary\nsolvent\ngradient\nusing\nthe following\nsolvents:\nSolvent\nA: 75:25 methanol: 0.5\nN ammonium\nacetate\n(v/v) and Solvent\nB: methanol.\nThis\nmethod\nis not recommended\nunder the present\nprotocols, and it will not be discussed further.\nDetermination\nof\nCarotenoid\nPigments\n1991):\na. separation\nof\nchlorophyll\nand carotenoid\npigments, however,\nit is also capable\nof separating\nthe major\nchlorophyll\nbreakdown products.\nThe precision\nof the HPLC\nmethod\nwas\nassessed by appropriate\ninternal\nstandard will increase precision.\n\nASSURANCE\n\nQuality\nassurance\n(QA) procedures\noutlined\nhere should\nbe routinely\nemployed\nto insure\naccurate,\nprecise\nand representative\nresults.\nA\nselected number of samples should be analyzed in\nduplicate (or triplicate) to assess representativeness\nand uncertainty\nin the method and instrumentation.\nSome fortified samples should be analyzed as part\nof the QA effort. Fortified samples are prepared in\nduplicate\nby\nspiking\na sample\nwith\nknown\nquantities\nof\nthe\nanalytes\nof\ninterest\nat\nconcentrations\nwithin the range expected\nin the\nsamples.\nFortified samples are used to assess the\nmethod\'s uncertainty\nin the presence\nof a typical\nsample matrix.\nIn addition,\nsystem and spiked\nblanks should be routinely\nanalyzed.\nA system\nblank\nconsists\nof a filter,\nreagents,\nand the\nglassware\nand hardware\nutilized in the analytical\nscheme.\nThe system blank is quantified\nunder\nidentical instrumental\nconditions as the samples and\nis analyzed by appropriate quantitative\nmethods.\nThe system blank may not contain any of the\nanalytes of interest above the MDL (see below) or\ncorrective\naction is taken.\nA spiked\nblank is\ndefined as a system blank plus an authentic external\nstandard containing\nthe analytes of interest.\nEach\nset of samples should be accompanied\nby a spiked\nblank and is quantified under the same instrumental\nconditions as the samples.\n\nA Cantha\n\nA;_,,.VE,,,,,,a\n\nthe\n\n13.4\nQUALITY\nPROCEDURES\n\n(Bidigare 1991).\nPrepare\npigment\nsamples\nfor injection\nby\nmixing a 1000 laL portion\nof the aqueous\nacetone pigment extract and 300 laL distilled\nwater, shake, and equilibrate for 5 min prior to\ninjection.\nInject the sample onto the HPLC\ncolumn.\nFollowing\ninjection\nof the sample onto the\nHPLC\nsystem,\nuse a gradient\nprogram\nto\noptimize\nthe separation\nof chlorophyll\nand\ncarotenoid\npigments. Bidigare\n& Remote\n\n2, David\nSensing,\n\nof Oceanography,\nUniversity\nHorn\n\nSan\n\n14.1 INTRODUCTION\nIn\naddition\nto\nHPLC\nanalyses,\nit\nis\nrecommended\nthat\nthe\nstandard\nfluorometric\nmethodology\nused for measuring\nchlorophylls\nand\npheopigments\nalso be applied\nto (i) the same\nextracted pigment samples used for HPLC analysis,\nand (ii) additional independent\nsamples. Analysis of\nfluorometric\nchlorophyll\na concentration\nis a far\nsimpler procedure\nthan HPLC analysis, especially\nat sea. On a given research cruise, therefore, it is\neconomically\nfeasible to acquire and process many\nmore fluorometric\nthan HPLC\nsamples\nand to\nstatistically\nrelate\nfluorometric\nand\nHPLC\nchlorophyll\na concentrations\nusing linear regression\nanalysis.\nThis additional analysis will also enable a\ndirect link to the historical\nbio-optical\nalgorithms\nand\ndatabase\ndevelopment\nduring\nthe CZCS\nvalidation experiments.\nProtocols for fluorometric\ndetermination\nof the\nconcentrations\nof chlorophyll\nand pheopigments\nwere developed\ninitially by Yentsch and Menzel\n(1963) and Holm-Hansen\net al. (1965), and are\ndescribed\nin detail\nby Strickland\nand Parsons\n(1972). Holm-Hansen\net al. (1965) and Strickland\nand\nParsons\n(1972)\nused\nfirst\nprinciples\nof\nfluorescence\nspectroscopy\nto\nderive\nthese\nfluorometric\nequations.\nThe equation proposed by\nYentsch\nand Menzel\n(1963)\nis only indirectly\nlinked\nto first\nprinciples,\nthrough\ndebatable\nassumptions,\nand its use is not recommended.\nAlthough these measurements\nhave been shown to\ncontain errors as compared to HPLC determinations\n(Trees et al. 1985; Smith et al. 1987; Hoepffner and\nSathyendranath\n1992; Bianchi et al. 1995; Tester et\nal.\n1995),\nthe CZCS\nphytoplankton\npigment\nconcentration\nalgorithms\nwere based\non them\nentirely.\nThe SeaWiFS protocols for this analysis\nwill be those given in Strickland\nand Parsons\n(1972) as updated by this protocol.\n\nDiego\n\nUniversity\n\nof Maryland\nPoint,\n\nM. Karl 2 and\n\nCenter\n\nLaurie\n\nState\n\nUniversity,\n\nof Hawaii,\nfor\n\nVan\n\nHeukelem\n\ns\n\nCalifornia\n\nHawaii\n\nEnvironmental\n\nScience,\n\nMaryland\nPigment\ndatabases\ngenerally\nshow\na lognormal distribution,\nwhich is consistent\nwith that\nproposed\nby Campbell\n(1995)\nfor bio-optical\nproperties.\nTherefore,\nit is appropriate\nto perform\nlog-linear regressions\non HPLC determined\ntotal\nchlorophyll\na (chlorophyllide\na, chlorophyll\na\nepimer,\nchlorophyll\na\nallomer,\nmonoviny!\nchlorophyll\na and divinyl\nchlorophyll\na) and\nfluorometrically\ndetermined\nchlorophyll,\nusing\nmodel I regressions.\nStandard Model I regressions\nwere selected\nbecause\nHPLC\ndetermined\ntotal\nchlorophyll\na concentrations\nare to be predicted\nfrom\nfluorometrically\ndetermined\nchlorophyll\n[Model\nI regressions\nare appropriate\nfor both\npredictions\nand\ndetermining\nfunctional\nrelationships,\nwhereas Model II regressions\nshould\nnot be used to predict values of y given x (page\n543,\nSokal and Rohlf,\n1995)].\nExamples\nof\nregression\nmodels\npredicting\nlog HPLC\ntotal\nchlorophyll\na (following\nChapter\n20\nHPLC\nprotocols)\nfrom log fluorometric\nchlorophyll\na for\nthree cruises\nin different\ngeographic\nareas are\nshown in Figures. 14.1, 14.2, and 14.3. There are\nstatistically\nsignificant differences,\nalthough for the\nGulf of California (GoCal November\n1996, Figure\n14.3) there\nseems\nto be a reasonably\ngood\nagreement.\nOne to one ratios have been found for\nother geographic\nareas, although\nit can have a\nseasonal cycle within each area.\nTherefore,\nthe\noffset between\nHPLC\ntotal chlorophyll\na and\nfluorometric\nchlorophyll\nmust be determined\nfor a\nselected number of samples for each cruise, so that\na scaling factor can be applied to other fluorometric\nsamples, if necessary.\nAbsorption\nof light in seawater,\nor any other\nmedium, is a volumetric\nprocess, even though the\nvolume absorption\ncoefficient\nmay vary with the\ndensity of the medium. For ocean color and optical\nanalyses, therefore, the concentration\nof chlorophyll\na shall be expressed\nin units of mass per unit\nvolume of seawater, either in I.tg L -I or mg m "3\n\n162\n\nOcean ptics rotocols\nO\nP\nForSatellite cean olor\nO\nC Sensor\nValidation\nThis differs from the JGOFS\nprotocols,\nwhich\nspecify that concentrations seawater\nin\nof\nchlorophylla and pheopigments\nshouldbe\nexpressed[.tg kg "1.\nin\n14.2 SAMPLING\nAND STORAGE\n\nfrom exposure\nto light.\nFor periods longer than\nseveral\nhours, the samples\nshould be stored in\nliquid nitrogen. Stabilization\ntime has\nto be the same for both pigment standards and for\nnatural\nsamples.\nTo minimize\nthis source\nof\nuncertainty,\nand to standardize\nthis measurement\ntechnique,\nit is recommended\nthat both acidified\nnatural sample and acidified pigment standards be\nallowed to react with the acid for one minute prior\nto recording the acidified fluorescence\nsignal (F_).\nTwo drops of 5% v/v hydrochloric\nac!d is added to\neach of the pigment standards and natural samples.\nOnce the acid is added, the sample in the test tube\nshould be mixed by inverting\nthe tube several\ntimes, using parafiim as a stopper.\nAll fluorometric\nmeasurements\nfor both pigment\nstandards\nand\nnatural samples\nshould be carried out at room\ntemperature.\nA 90% acetone blank 03111) and a\nacidified\nacetone\nblank\n(Bika) should\nalso be\n\npigment\nstandards.\nThe\nstock\nchlorophyll\na\nstandard,\nwith its concentration\nmeasured\non a\nspectrophotometer\nas described\nabove, should be\ndiluted\nusing calibrated\ngas-tight\nsyringes,\nand\nClass A volumetric\npipettes\nand flasks.\nThe\nminimum number of dilutions of the stock standard\nfor calibrating\na fluorometer\ndepends on whether it\nis a digital model (Turner Designs 10-AU-005),\nor\nit is an analog model with a mechanical mode (e.g.\nTurner Designs 10-005) for changing sensitivity. A\nminimum of 5 dilutions is required for calibrating a\ndigital fluorometer.\nFluorometers\nwith a variety of\ndoor settings, such as the Turner Designs Model\n10-005, must be calibrated\nfor each door setting\nusing at least three standard\nconcentrations\nper\ndoor. This instrument\nwas recently beta-tested\nusing samples collected at\nthe US JGOFS Hawaii Ocean Time-series\nStation\nALOHA\n(22.75\xc2\xb0N,\n158\xc2\xb0W).\nAn annual\nset of\nmonthly pigment samples (0-200 m) was analyzed\nby HPLC using the protocols described in Chapter\n20.\nExtracts\nwere\nsubsequently\ndiluted\nand\nanalyzed\n\nwith\n\nthe\n\nTD-700.\n\nModel\n\nOcean Color Sensor\n\nValidation\n\nanalyses, which summarize\nthese results are given\nbelow (pigment concentrations\nare expressed\nas ng\nL-l).\nHPLC Chl a = 0.80[TD-700\n\nChl a] + 25.98 (r2 = 0.916),\n\nHPLC Chl b = 0.80[TD-700\n\nChl b] + 11.74 (rz = 0.843),\n\nHPLC Chl c = 1.21 [TD-700 Chl c] + 3.40 (r2 = 0.867).\nIt is interesting noteworthy that the TD-700 did\nnot detect pheopigments\nin any of the samples\nanalyzed.\n\nREFERENCES\nBianchi, T.
